RATING: 10
Gameplay is simple: You must place three houses on the land that your drawn card is. If you already have a house touching that land type, you must place a house there. However, the ways to score points vary each game [through three randomly selected cards at set-up] as well as the map varies from each play. So one game you might be trying to cluster your houses together and place them near the mountains. Another game you might be trying to have your houses in small clusters and going in a straight line across the map. 
There are special locations on the map that you can build near to give you additional building or moving powers each turn. These abilities can help mitigate the luck factor from whatever card you drew that turn. Most of these special features are touching two or more different land types and often have two locations on the map so if you want to obtain a particular power, you usually are able to if you aim for them early on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]tl;dr - This is a fun game with more strategy than is first apparent, and it's worth the time to learn how its systems work. When players know what they're doing, this is a unique and tense area control game with interesting scoring objectives that change every game.[/i]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

Kingdom Builder has gotten a bad rap, and it's a controversial game no matter who you ask. I happen to enjoy it, and here's why: 

Though the game appears simple, that's deceptive and there's a good deal of strategy underlying it. My friend described it as "Go meets Carcassonne" and I think that's a good summary. You are constrained by what card you draw (which, yes, is random), but if you play well and take advantage of the bonuses on the table, you will be able to mitigate this constraint, and this is where the game forces you to be creative and adaptive as you go. 

Skilled play will always beat random play, and it's great fun and a superb test of the brain. I highly recommend it, but do make sure you try it before you buy it, as it can be polarizing. I've appreciated continuing to play the game, as the random setup provides new scoring cards, new special abilities, and a new map every single time. If you play the game an awful lot, you may want to look at an expansion, but as far as I'm concerned, Donald X. Vaccarino considered the game complete as is. 

Sure, there's not much of a theme, but the mechanics are pleasing enough that you can paper over the lack of a theme, especially in a game that can crank out in about fifteen minutes per player. I love the variability and modular board, and I really think this game provides you with a range of deep strategic options.

RATING: 7
This is from the same designer as Dominion.  It's an interesting little strategy game that's very simple to pick up.

With 8 different board cards and a variety of victory cards, every game is unique, similar to Dominion.  However, there's a bit more luck involved in KB than Dominion, and I just don't find myself getting into this one nearly as much. 

The other downside is that the game becomes significantly longer the more players there are.  Since the end of the game is predicated upon one player placing all 40 settlements, this simply means that a 4-player game is twice as long as a 2-player game. 

Even with the negatives, this is a good game, but if given a choice, I'd normally pick other strategy games over it.

RATING: 4
If there's one thing Donald X. knows how to do, it's pander to the lowest common denominator among Eurogamers.  Kingdom Builder is yet another example of that.  It's similar in game play to games like Through the Desert and Paris Connection, but like all bad Euro game stereotypes it limits the player iteraction -- the thing that makes those other games fun -- and replaces it with a competition against the game system.

To play the game you draw a card and place markers where the card tells you to.  You can gamble that you will always draw the exact card you need, or you can play to minimize the negative affects of a bad card draw.  Or you can just play Paris Connection.

RATING: 7

LIKE: fun placing houses, blocking opponents. many ways to score. simple rules, some depth, high replayability.
DISLIKE: getting blocked. there is some luck in the card draw. I suspect the maps could be "solved" for optimum strategy after a while.

OVERALL: surprisingly fun game. it could be quicker (5p=2h), and some luck is in the cards drawn, but that can be mitigated. It's the power tiles that make the game.

RATING: 1.1
This game reminds me of Connect Four with a crappy theme tacked on to it to give that certain "Euro Elegance."

You're not really building a kingdom.  You are placing cubes.  You know what this game needs?  War!  What better way to build your kingdom than to destroy your neighbor's kingdom and lay siege to his castle and pillage his villages!  And you know what be even better?  Dinosaurs.  Yeah, add a herd of triceratops stampeding across the landscape goring his people (cubes).  But I'm not playing with cube dinosaurs.  I need plastic dinosaurs.

If you did this, then yeah, Kingdom Builder would be a good game.  Unfortunately, it is not a good game.

RATING: 5
I played this game just twice, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

After my first play of Kingdom Builder, I discovered that I had played a major rule wrong. That explained why I hated the game so much the first time. Then I got a chance to play it a second time and, while I still don't really like the game, I can now see its merits a little bit better and understand how it won the SpJ in 2012.

Kingdom Builder does come indeed with some strengths: for one thing, the quality of artwork and components is pretty good and everything looks nice on the table. Also the game is very quick to teach and easy to understand, which will make the game probably really accessible for kids.

On the other hand though, I'm really not a fan of its game-play. As many reviews mentioned already, I also don't like the fact that you are dealt one card and that for that turn you're completely stuck with it... so when you don't need to build over there, well, it sucks to be you! Yet what I found more annoying is another rule: the fact that you have to build adjacent to one of your buildings. I know that you can work your way around this restriction, but at some point during the game it's the game itself that is basically making most decisions... not you! I find this extremely frustrating and very, very stupid. Without this rule I'm pretty positive I would actually enjoy the game much much more.

So... while I don't hate Kingdom Builder anymore, I'm surely still not its biggest fan. I see why some people might like it though and how it could be a successful product for a family crowd. Just probably not for me.

RATING: 9
[With the first three expansions, plus some Queenies]

"Old school" spatial abstract in some ways, but with "powers" and lots of variant scoring.  Not too confrontational, usually, and interesting.

Really darn well done.  Highly under-rated.  Vaccarino is one of the great designers of our age.

With the expansions, there is nearly as much variety as Dominion here.

RATING: 7.6
:cry: :D :D :) Update after 20+ plays: a great 30 minute light game. Scratches lots of my favorite itches: spatial reasoning, area control, making the most of a tough situation. 

There is a luck factor but if you plan for flexibility, and plan your power tiles well, you can usually mitigate it at least 95% of the time. 

Liked it so much that I kick-started the Big Box Edition. 

People hate on this and Carcassonne, but they are the absolute two BEST games in their weight class, and it ain't even close.

RATING: 5
This is actually a decent strategy game: yes, there's luck in it, but very little, and certainly not anywhere near enough to swing a game. 

So why have I given at a mere 5? It starts with the theme: I tried to lift up the game by the theme, and the game fell to the floor, leaving the flimsy theme dangling in my hands. It is an abstract, pure and simple. As a fan of theme, I feel somewhat insulted that, rather than present a fairly decent abstract game, they pretended Kingdom Builder was something it is not.

Purely on Kingdom Builder's qualities as an abstract, it is a decent game; but there are soooo many abstracts out there that are far, far better. This one works; flawlessly, in fact. But it's dry as dust. You'd be much better off with Ingenious or Pente rather than this poser.

RATING: 8
I love this game. So many options provided by boards and cards means no two games are the same - it plays fast and is easy to teach new players. Always happy to play this even when I know I usually lose. The player is at the mercy of what terrain card he draws so you need luck as well as strategy but the game is short so even if you have bad luck it does not drag on.
Having now played this with expansion cards and 5 players I find the gap slows up a bit as people have more options. The base game is clean and fast.
Over the years I have downgraded this game from 10 because it's not so much fun against dedicated players who can leave you standing and then it's not some much fun when you are behind with no way back

RATING: 6
Read the endgame scoring cards and then study the map carefully to spot the best scoring locations. Figure out which bonus action tiles will be the most help.  Place your pieces to get those bonus actions and maximize your score.  Of course, the other players may get there ahead of you and the luck of the card draw that determines the terrain you can place on can shaft you.  In our game, one player drew a long sequence of desert cards, while I never saw a flowers one.

For me, Kingdom Builder felt quite abstract and quite flat.  Interaction there is, in that an opponent can block you out of a location, but the board is so big that none of that ever felt decisive; there's always somewhere else to place and score.

I do like the variability of multiple modular boards, randomised victory conditions and bonus action tiles.  Production is up to the usual Queen standards, though the artwork is bland and generic.

RATING: 8
Many people have given Kingdom Builder a bad rep. But we just don't see why. Yes it lacks the innovation and strategy of Dominion. But it is still fun for what it is. And that is an abstract strategy game with beautiful art and fairly simple mechanisms. You draw a card and then using the terrain on that card play your houses onto the modular board to the best of your ability. Vaccarino has made a game that you can teach in about 5 minutes (that's how long it took a couple to teach us to play it at Dice Tower Con 2014) but enjoy playing for a very long time.

[user=mst3k4ever][/user]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A great game that often gets wrongfully accused of not giving the player options, due to the one card rule and the rule about building next to an existing settlement if you are able. The game is a puzzle that requires players to think about how to use the "powers" to break away from the games' restrictions. Positioning your settlements properly and using your "powers" creatively is key to victory.

The game is light, but players still make interesting, strategic decisions throughout, assuming they set themselves up that way.

One downside is that the game can go south for you on the second turn if you are unlucky with the terrain card you draw that turn. After one turn, you may not have been able to set yourself up for flexibility. If you are unlucky with your second card draw and are forced to build your three settlements next to your current settlements, it can make you play catch up for the remainder of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This is good right out of the box but I would definitely suggest picking up the expansions as well.  They add a lot more replayability to an already good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent strategic game of settlement expansion, with a high quality finish. Three victory conditions are chosen from a possible ten at random every game, and there are double the amount of board pieces you need to play the game, so every game can be different. The victory conditions work to significantly alter your approach to the game, rather than feeling tacked on. Each game board has specific pieces you can pick up to gain an extra play every turn, or move in a way that you usually wouldn't be able to, and the use of cards to determine the type of area you must play in works well for this kind of game.

The game is nice and short, and only needs five minutes to set up. Plays best with 4 or 5 players. Two or three players would also work, but it probably gives too much room to play and means you can always place your settlements where you want.

Great longevity, quick to pick up, and has plenty of strategy. If anything, it's a little dry - it's a game that my group enjoy and play occasionally, but it doesn't generate any laughs or conversation afterwards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

(Surprisingly, to me) I enjoyed my one play of this.

Still may be a bit too abstract for my tastes...

RATING: 7
Kingdom Builder is an almost abstract, quite fast-playing and almost gateway game.
It has very simple rules, but also a number of nuances, even if it's a short game: every turn the players aim for short term tactical placement, while setting up their position for future plays.
I am a little annoyed by the single terrain card drawn every turn: I can see it being a decisive factor in determining the winner...
Anyway, Kingdom Builder is another polished and intriguing design by Donald X...  yet it suffers from the tragic destiny of being compared to the most innovative and paradigm-changing design of the last 10 years: Dominion.

Update february 27, 2013: with more than two players the game is definitely more enjoyable. The board is more crowded and there's more blocking. It is a fast paced but thoughtful filler. Vote raised to 7.

RATING: 6
Essen '11: Feels like Domaine with variable victory conditions. Players take turns placing influence markers on a terrain-laden board, blocking off sections a la Domaine. There are placement restrictions based on terrain type. Placing next to certain unique locations give a player access to special placement abilities for the rest of the game. Each game uses a random set of end game victory point conditions. The game ends when a player runs out of influence markers, and players tally victory points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I know this is a game that many people are divided on, so I was excited to try before buying to make up my own mind. I could definitely see the similarities to Dominion. The variable setup with the boards and goals could lead to hundreds of unique games. Then there is the whole idea of building towards the special abilities first and then making the switch to going after the victory points later. I'm a huge Dominion fan, so this was definitely a winner. You can easily see that the game is an ideal family game (which is right up my alley) because the rules are pretty basic. I think the strategies might be a little tough for some non-gamers to understand right away, but with a few games under their belt it should work fine. I don't think the limited options (only 1 card per turn) is actually a problem in the game. It limits AP, and forces players to plan ahead based on how many terrains they border. So, while that is often one of the primary complaints, I actually think it's one of the selling features.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Forget the theme, this is a completely abstract game, probably best described as Go with colour. Easy to learn/teach and play, but the variable map and end goals keep the game fresh, such that each session presents a new puzzle to solve. Requires a house rule for people to not be screwed out of the game on turn 2, also the early decisions are vastly more important than the ones near the end, so the game is a bit front loaded.

People who claim that this game runs on autopilot, likely don't get how you need to play around your handsize limitation of one card.

RATING: 5
Just not a very good game.  They took a simple game concept--which is fine, I like simple game concepts; I like a lot of you-have-one-type-of-move games--and threw on a bunch of varying powers and varying endgame goals.  If you look at it that way, it is actually very much like Dominion.

The problem is that the 'simple game mechanic' just isn't very interesting or really even any good.  You very often have 2 or less options of play choices on a turn, and really have no choice beyond just simply picking whichever choice gives you more points in the randomly drawn end game goals.

Plus I think the special actions are way too powerful, and I mean all of the place-an-extra ones and all in the same way.  From the handful of games I've played, whichever player gets more special-action spaces which in effect gives them more settlement placements per turn wins simply because they end up placing many more settlements than their opponents by the end of the game.

Bleh.

RATING: 3
Provisional rating based on one play, likely to change if I play it again (probably going down!).

Felt to me like Through the Desert - this is a route building game, rather than area control or building a kingdom (which makes the name totally misleading!)

You're chaining to points of victory - but unlike through the desert you have a distinct lack of control, and because of this and the mechanics it can disolve into AP hell ...

Very tactical - you have the start of the game analysis where you are going to place and your first turn, then after that it is a case of optimising your current turn, which you can try to plan in advance, but can be messed up (inadvertently as no one will know what card you have)

I found the whole thing dull - it plays slower than Through the Desert, is more fiddly, you have a lot less control (getting the 'wrong' card is frustrating) doesn't have cool plastic camels. The game is solving the optimisation puzzle of the card you draw, but I just didn't find it enjoyable.

The theme is totally pasted on and doesn't really fit - why am I building these chains of settlements? Why does getting to this spot let me now build more settlements in rows? Why does the knight score me vps for building in rows? 

More than happy to see it go back in the box and didn't really care who'd won at that point, not a good sign.

Lots of variety, with each game having a different puzzle to solve, different vp cards and variable map (don't know why they didn't just back print the maps though and have a separate scoring board).

I'm sure many euro-gamers will love it. I don't.

RATING: 7
Kingdom Builder is a fairly light strategy game, wherein a random map and victory conditions are generated, and players must claim areas in a fashion that nets them the most victory points. Players draw a terrain card each turn to determine which type of terrain they can claim for the turn. Sometimes, a player can wait an entire game to get a particular terrain card, but it doesn't happen. This can result in some disappointing games, but clever players will find a way to work around this. The game is fairly quick, and good fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
3 plays in a row.  Light placement game that has good player interaction.  Contrary to some of the negative comments I feel that this game does have some interesting decisions to make.  It isn't Agricola, nor does it pretend to be anything other than what it is..a good fun game.

More plays now under the belt and I am even more impressed with the possibilities in the game.  Each game plays very differently due to the variable setup and the variable victory conditions. Definitely underrated.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Kingdom Builder is a short, light, mostly tactical experience with high replayability.  The game provides variability in both map construction (which affects which special powers will be available to the players) and in how victory points will be scored.  This creates a highly variable game in which both the goals and the means to achieve the goals change each game.  The meat of the game is in figuring out how the special powers and goals interact and then executing a strategy as best as your cards allow you to.  

The actual gameplay is simple, and I think this is where some get frustrated by this title.  Draw a card, and play 3 houses on the terrain you drew, and use special powers you have acquired.  This tight constraint on where one can play makes the game feel more light than it really is.  After a few plays, though, players will begin to see how to position themselves to make use of whatever the deck provides.  This would be rather dull if not for the fact that the special powers and goals and map change from game to game - it is this variation that keeps the house positioning interesting.  

This is a light game that plays quickly - 45 min is probably accurate once you know the game, and 30 min is achievable with quick players.  Interaction is limited, but can have a significant impact.  Turns are mostly tactical optimization of point scoring for the landscape drawn with just a few strategic considerations.  My wife really enjoys it, and I suspect we will get a large number of plays out of it before it gets stale - and then there's expansions.  

Update: After 15 plays, we both really enjoy this and consider it a must own.  Short, and easy to teach, it is a great game for casual gamers, with enough there for me to enjoy as well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Juego abstracto, con el tema pegado. Se supone que tenemos que construir reinos, aunque lo suyo en realidad es buscar emplazamientos para las casas. En tu turno, tienes que sacar una carta y tienes que colocar las casas en territorios del tipo indicado en la carta, con la particularidad de que, si es posible, tienes que estar adyacente a tus propias casas ya construidas. 
En cada partida se sacan unas cartas "de puntuación" que te dicen qué cosas se van a tener en cuenta para obtener puntos, lo que le da mucha rejugabilidad.
Es un juego ligero, sencillo de jugar pero con profundidad suficiente como para estrujarte el cerebro. Me gustan ese estilo de juegos.
Es apto para no jugones.

RATING: 8
This game seems underwhelming at first, then frustrating and when finally it clicks, it and can be a very interesting not very long abstract game.

At first it seems underwhelming, because it looks like  you do not have a lot of options, it looks like there is no strategy. Then it can be very frustrating when you get stuck in a type of terrain in an area of the board because you did not place very carefully your first houses. It is also very frustrating when someone gets extra placements each turn from special powers and ends the game when you have many houses remaining.

Finally, you will learn from your mistakes, understand how to place your initial houses, how to avoid getting stuck and how important special powers are and how to get them.

The problem of this game is it can be very difficult to introduce new players to it, because they can think that the game is bland or frustrating or maybe they do not know the game as well as you do and cannot present an interesting match.

RATING: 7
I enjoy this one, but it's not without issues. Primarily, while it's short, it's probably not short enough - games seem like they'll be decided in the first 5 minutes or so. For a light game, too much AP potential also. And you have to be willing to play really fast once you know you've lost, otherwise the game will take too long.

Despite its issues though, I do like it. I like the variety, I like the reasonably quick playing time, I like that it does have some interesting tactical nuance. But I doubt there is much long-term value here, and I can't see anything like the same scope for expansion as the deck-builders have. While Kingdom Builder isn't a deck-building game, it presses many of the same buttons.

RATING: 8
Played it 3 times in a row straight out of the box, and still would have been happy to play more, which is a very good sign!  The game plays quickly enough (our games were taking 30 minutes) and has an addictive quality along with variance from game to game that definitely gives it that "one more time!" feel.

Just from a rules read, Kingdom Builder might sound pretty boring and a bit simplistic.  However, while it's definitely not a heavy game by any means, the randomized selection of special actions and scoring cards from game to game is a fun puzzle to try to navigate from game to game.

The main complaint I can see people having with this game is that card draws can sometimes end up dictating your game since you only have a hand of 1 card and must play to that terrain type.  I personally didn't mind this, as the special actions and shrewd placement can help to mitigate the luck of the draw.  However, some people are not going to care for this.  Fortunately, the game is very easily house-ruled is it's a problem for you.  The third game we played, we played with a hand of 2 rather than 1, and while I would play either way, some of the players liked the game much better with a hand of two.

Overall, Kingdom Builder is a great mix of simplicity and fun.  You could easily teach it to anyone in less than 5 minutes, but the game play will keep you coming back again and again!

[thing=107529][/thing] is a 2011 Spiel release by [thing=36218][/thing] designer [person=10525][/person].  It was one of the most popular games at the convention, as it is a perfect design for such events: simple rules, quick playtime, high variability, and fairly addictive. [thing=107529][/thing] feels like a good idea that settled for "good enough." RATING: 10
The variable set-up alters your approach to the game every time, while the interaction between the players offers up subtle questions as to whether or not one should block them, or even, force them to expand where they would rather not expand to.

Also, your first placements are extremely important, as you have to judge how to get the most points based off the geography (bottlenecks are important to control), the point cards, and the special powers.

While the game seems like it should be difficult to teach with all the variables that change from game to game, it is actually quite easy as you only have to explain the variables for this game, as opposed to all the possible variables, so you learn as you go instead of all at once.

Nomads offers some mild twists that increase the explosive possibilities of a player's turn, while showcasing some new ways to get points. It improves a great game more than I thought it would.

RATING: 8
[u]April 2014:[/u] I must say I have never EVER felt so underwhelmed after reading a rulebook. I just could not figure out how thi game could be of any interest whatsoever. I mean come on, you have to put three houses by turn and you don't even get to choose where??? wtf??? I almost traded it away without even playing it. But I did try it (just by principle) and guess what... That game is actually really good! There is, sure, a very big constraint on what you are allowed to do with the card draw which seems like it kills all choice but actually no, there many places where one could place three houses on green pastures. You also have flexibility from the powers you can gain and you actually need to be far-sighted so that long term strategy is actually present and not in a small dose! Indeed, given you are allowed to build houses only adjacent to each other except if you can't means you always try at the beginning to not open up your territory (so that chances are you won't be able to play adjacent and can therefore play wherever you want) but in the middle game where you'll presumably be always able to do so then you're actively trying to open up at each of your little villages so that each time you can choose which village to expand. This was a very pleasant surprise and was our most played game of 2012. The game is good at every count and the different objectives and powers really make some very different games from one to the other. Again, I was very pleasantly surprised. The Nomads expansion provides some additional variation without modifying the gameplay much.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

It plays so fast that you'll often want to try it again right after the first game, but with new boards and new scoring cards.

RATING: 8
This game gets a certain amount of hate largely because its designer didn't make Dominion 2.0. For that matter, it would have likely been hailed as a classic had it appeared years earlier, with the likes of Carcassonne.

However...

Gamers who come to the table for a gentle, thoughtful, artful experience will enjoy its variability and regard its supposedly greatest weak point--the fact that you must play your pieces according to the one card you draw--as a virtue.

This game plays on the idea that restriction is a key component to creativity--something that artists often understand more than audiences.

RATING: 6
[i]Received this after winning the Queen Lancaster Games contest.[/i]

OK, so I got this game for free, so how picky can I be, right? :) I rated this game a 7 after one play. After reading the rules, I expected a slightly different take on Through the Desert, and after one play, that seems to be about right... with some caveats. Through the Desert is very pure in its tension. There's very few rules, and all you do on a turn is place two camels--that's it. It's clean, and it's full of tension.

With Kingdom Builder, you have a bit more going on. There's the restrictions of placing only in the terrain of the random card you're dealt. And, that can cause a healthy dose of frustration, which will bother some players but not others. You've got an increasing number of building placements if you can reach those special locations on the board. And, you've got the "every game will play different" design intent with only 4 out of 8 building types and 3 out of 10 victory point conditions in any given game.

That last point is not trivial; I expect that each game will feel noticeably different and present a twist on the general challenge. My rating will likely change after a handful of plays to see if this holds true. All in all, it's nothing spectacular, but it's a solid game with some "meat" and a reasonable playing time. It's a great "next step" for those having played Ticket to Ride.

Update after a couple of two player games--the two player game is definitely more "polite." Right now, I'd say this is best with 4, then 3, then 2. Maybe we can come up with more cutthroat tactics eventually, but with only half the pieces in a two player game, there's plenty of room to work around obstacles.

Update: Traded away in a math trade for Through the Ages. Ultimately, I was playing this mostly with my wife, but it's not a very good two player game. And, given the collective "meh" reaction from my game group, I just didn't see this getting any regular play.

RATING: 7
I was on the fence about buying this for a long time. The game is polarizing and I didn't want to end up on the wrong side of this discussion. It turned out that my money and time investment paid out. I really enjoy the game, although it's easy to understand why some people do not.

It's utterly different from Dominion, another Spiel des Jahres winner by Donald X. Vaccarino. Dominion is a simple, but very smart game you can lose yourself in. For a Spiel des Jahres winner it is maybe a little bit too smart. Kingdom Builder is more suitable for a Spiel des Jahres game. RATING: 6.5
In collection since 2016
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Kingdom Builder is probably the best take on the basic German route building game that I've played since Through the Desert, which is pretty high praise. Just when I was afraid Donald X was going to turn into the next Klaus-Jurgen Wrede, he's instead morphing into a Teuber, in other words, a man well known for one franchise, but with other tricks up his sleeve.

Most basically, KB features a board made up of hexes depicting various types of terrain. Each round, players draw a card that has one of those terrain types and must place 3 houses onto that terrain type. The trick is that if possible, you must place pieces adjacent to existing ones, but if you plan things out, you can fill up areas and then jump to others.

More interestingly, the game rewards players differently each time around based on 3 scoring cards (of a deck of 10) that change what you score at the end of the game. Connecting to shiny silver castles is always desirable, but scoring cards can give you bonuses for spreading out horizontally, connecting to bonus chit areas, or the like.

The aforementioned bonus chits are the other interesting wrinkle. Placing houses adjacent to these spots gives the first two players special powers that they get to use each round. Some of them are simple...i.e.: place a free house from your supply onto a desert space each round. Others are much more interesting and difficult to use correctly, such as the "move one house two spaces in a straight line each round" or "move one house from anywhere onto the board to any water space each round." The special chits vary based on the board, as the board is modular and made up of 4 out of 8 panels each game.

Though this is fairly simple on the surface, the special power chits and the need to evaluate the most efficient way to score each game makes this a real winner in my book. It almost completely obsoletes Through the Desert for me, and I thought that that was already a very solid game, albeit one that tends to get samey with repeat play. KB, by contrast, seems like it should have a much longer life because of the varied board set-up, varied special powers, and varied scoring.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
High time to add a comment. When Kingdom Builder (KB for short) was announced, I politely ignored it: Queen Games rarely strike a chord with me these days, and to make it worse the game even won the SdJ. Ugh, no, thanks. And thus that situation persisted until one day someone at a club made me and my partner play it: there were a few rules wrong, but we weren't really won over in any way. Not bad, not good, nice artwork, very limited action space due to the single card draw. A few weeks later we played again at a gaming friend's house, 'cementing' my views that the game was too random to be fun. So I continued politely ignoring the game again. 

Not so my partner, who kinda-liked it and wondered for how much it was to be had. Well, Amazon to the rescue: for very little indeed. To the point where she didn't mind spending the money and playing some more. The fact that it came with the first expansion helped considerably. So the box arrived, I break out the rules, read through them again, and we were off. The first game wasn't very pleasant, but in the second I suddenly noticed that you could 'steer' where your options lay in a very subtle manner my previous cursory glances did not make clear. By carefully expanding towards certain areas you could sometimes 'trick' the game into allowing you an essentially free placement of a house in an area... which would net you a good position with respect to the goal cards, or with respect to the extra action tiles. All of a sudden all sorts of vague ideas clicked into place, and I realised I was sitting behind a fairly unique game of area control (which almost never fails to captivate me), featuring varying end goals (thus guaranteeing play variability), yet rulewise light enough to be played with non-gamers while remaining interesting in a friendly, soothing way. Oooooookaaaaayyyyy. I've been playing KB with pleasure since, which can be rightly called an impressive achievement. It's not often a game manages to win me over like so.

The real clinger came when a friend, adamant KB hater, wondered whether this time I had truly lost my marbles, and asked me why on Earth I'd bought that crap. Like me, he had played the game only once or twice without full knowledge of how to actually place houses effectively. So I showed him. He now owns more KB games and expansions than me. 

KB is by no means a complex game, or a difficult one, or even a challenging one. Game-technically it is weak, with the area control and goal cards forming the most interesting aspects. But it simply [i]works[/i] without once feeling overly artificial. The rules are simple and straightforward, yet winning isn't always easy given the fickle card draw. But that is, for once, okay. If there are disadvantages, I'd say there are two: 
— Sometimes the card draw indeed [i]is[/i] very terribly lopsided. As in 'drawing four or five similar terrains in a row'. (Not less!) There is nothing you can do about this, and you will lose the game because of it. I wish that the game would be hardened a tad against this scenario.
— The base game comes with too little variation in the game goals. For more, you need to purchase expansions. This is completely unnecessary and only serves up to rack up a good purchase bill.

For the rest a solid and dependable lightweight title remains. I'm going to be a little lenient and award the game a 7 because the expansions, money sinks as they are, do give the same extra legs. Otherwise it'd have been a 6.

RATING: 9
Recent plays with the group (all first-timers except me) cemented this in my top-10.

Light-medium strategic with random card draw and a touch of cutthroat screwage. Fun!

Enjoyable from the first draw, some frustration if your cards don't come up, but oh well - change your plans, use your tokens to their best tactical play, make do.

I lost the last game of the night by 1 pt ... and loved it. Such a close match where the final score is almost a surprise is refreshing. Instead of spending all this time gloating or being miserable about being in last place, you're out placing your guys to maximize your position ... then spend 3 minutes scoring, and whomever wins, wins!

Very pleasant experience. This is the fourth group I've introduced to - a hit everytime (including wife and kids). The varying map, powers and win conditions are fantastic - each game is fresh and each setup had a completely unique feel.

RATING: 8.5
Unique game of tactics
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Components: Simple wooden settlements. Gorgeous board, with a few little extraneous details on the hexes to give them personality. I like the pasted on fantasy theme, but would enjoy it more if the settlements were something a bit more mobile, like horses; it doesn't make sense to have them flying all over the place.

Experience: It's fun. It's a challenge to reach the various superpowered locations, to meet the victory conditions, and to allow for maximum maneuverability. It's best to not be adjacent to a particular terrain type, but failing that, it's best to be adjacent to as many of that type as possible, to create the most options.

It's fast. Games have been coming in at an hour each so far. We're partly slowed down by having to help each other recognize and adhere to the adjacency rule, which for some reason is really tough to wrap our brains around.

It's highly modular. The intersection of different map configurations, different location powers, and different victory conditions help to keep this game interesting. We can play several games back-to-back, and each has a distinctive feel to it, with unique struggles. I love the opening of games like this (e.g., Settlers of Catan, Thunderstone) where I survey the land, trying to recognize the various vulnerabilities to be exploited.

There are a few combinations of victory conditions that really complement each other (Knights + Citizens), and some that don't (Citizens + Hermit), so part of the strategy is identifying how the objectives overlay on the board, and which locations will be of most use. The location tiles are mostly balanced, except for the boats. Boats are incredibly useful, regardless of the victory condition, and there are half as many of them as there are other location tiles. In a recent game, I was able to use my first two turns to encircle the boats location, locking out all the other players, which gave me a huge advantage; and their card draws prevented them from stopping me. Horse stables might be too powerful as well.

Luck of the draw can be damning, but the game is so much fun! Games tend to be very close, which might mean it's just a matter of luck.

Susceptible to analysis paralysis, and one player getting shut out of critical locations.

First Played: 2011-11-22
Last Played: 2017-04-03
Plays (33):
2 players: 5
3 players: 15
4 players: 12
5 players: 1

RATING: 4
Een beurt bestaat uit één bouwactie en eventueel extra acties indien u bepaalde tegels heeft verzameld. U krijgt bij aanvang van het spel één terreinkaart in handen, u speelt deze in het begin van u beurt uit, plaatst drie huisjes op het overeenkomstige landschapstype volgens de bouwregels (zie onder) en trekt een nieuwe terreinkaart. Indien u een extra-actie-tegel heeft, mag u deze actie ook nog uitvoeren. Zo gauw een speler al z'n huisjes op het bord heeft staan, wordt de ronde uitgespeeld en wint de speler met de meeste punten. Punten scoort u door te voldoen aan de 3 opengelegde kingdomkaarten en door uw huisjes naast een kasteel te plaatsen.

Bouwregels :
- er kan slechts één huisje op een veld staan
- je kan enkel bouwen op gras, woestijn, bloemenveld, bos en dal
- je moet altijd bouwen aan eerder gebouwde huisjes indien mogelijk

RATING: 8
[2011-11-07 Rating: n.a. -> 8]

After reading the rules, I didn't really know what to think of Kingdom Builder. I was under the impression that it would be a bit simplistic and not very engaging. But I had the same feeling when I read the rules for Dominion the first time and since that turned out to be one of my most played games, I bought Kingdom Builder without a demo game at the Spiel 2011.

As it turns out, this was a good decision. Kingdom Builder is a not too heavy game in which you try to build a network of settlements that earn you as much gold as possible. The special actions and variable victory conditions really spice things up as every turn has a puzzle feel to it when figuring out where to place your settlements in order to earn the most gold. Sure, there is luck in the card draw and you can't plan every detail, but in this regard Kingdom Builder feels similar to Dominion. By acquiring certain special actions, you lay the foundations for a strategy. It might not work as planned if you don't draw the right cards, but by using the special actions in a clever way, there is almost never a really bad card draw.

To sum it up: Every person I introduced to Kingdom Builder enjoyed the game. It can be explained in five minutes, it plays fast (~10-15 minutes per player) and the game play will keep you coming back again and again.

RATING: 9
J'en suis à une bonne centaine de parties et franchement ce jeu est très bon dans son genre ! 

La réalisation est impeccable : le plateau est très coloré, toutes les cases sont bien lisibles et les cartes et les tuiles sont plutôt réussies. En revanche, l'immersion n'est pas un modèle du genre, mais on se prend très vite au jeu quand même. 

Kingdom Builder est principalement un jeu de placement, parfois de majorité, mais toujours un jeu d'optimisation. 
A votre tour, vous devez construire ou déplacer des bâtiments selon certaines règles en cherchant à optimiser leurs valeurs tout au long de la partie. Des actions Bonus viennent s'ajouter à votre action de base. Des cartes Personnages définissent des conditions particulières pour marquer des points avec vos bâtiments en fin de partie. 

Le tirage au sort des quadrants de jeu et surtout des 3 cartes Personnages du royaume (sur les 10 disponibles) est un vrai suspens. A peine révélés, on est déjà en train de voir comment agencer les combos des actions Bonus pour optimiser le score en fonction des cartes Personnages. Chaque joueur cherche à faire le max de points en faisant pas mal de sales coups aux autres. 

Dès la fin de ma première partie j'ai eu envie d'en faire une autre immédiatement, puis une autre, puis encore une autre... Kingdom Builder est bien plus subtile qu'il en a l'air et est très accrocheur, pour peu que vous aimiez ce type de jeu. Il est familial mais comblera aussi, je pense, les joueurs calculateurs. Les parties s'enchaînent rapidement. 

Il est en outre très facile de s'inventer de nouvelles cartes Personnages pour varier encore plus le plaisir, déjà énorme devant la très grande rejouabilité. Ce sera sans doute l'objet de futures extensions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Lots of people seem to not like this game because of what it isn't. It's not Dominion. If you want to play a Dominion-like game, may I suggest you play Dominion? If you want a lighter, quick playing placement game that works well in a family setting, this one is lots of fun. There's more subtlety and difficult choices than might be apparent at first glance. Also complaints about lack of interactivity don't seem well founded to me: you should watch how your opponents are seeking to optimize their scoring and you should try to deny them those opportunities where possible. Kingdom Builder is not the be-all and end-all of strategy gaming, but I like it for what it is.

RATING: 7
Players are expanding their kingdoms across a hex map of differing terrain.  Simple game where players reveal a terrain card, and must place three of their 40 houses on that terrain - one house (only) per hex.  As houses touch certain hexes, bonus action tiles are earned, allowing extra placements per turn, accelerating the game.  The game ends when someone has placed all their houses, and scores are tallied based on 3 scoring cards randomly dealt from a deck of 10 at the beginning of the game.  Very simple game in terms of mechanics, but has a pleasant "meaty filler" feel to it - plays fast, makes you think a little, a little bit of risk and luck involved, but doesn't make you flip the table in anger if it doesn't come off!  The eight map boards and different scoring card combinations keep things interesting and re-playable.  Worth a look.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
In theory this sounds like a pretty fun abstract tile-laying game: placing down houses on a modular board with different goals and actions each game. I assume that you know the rules/concept somewhat.

The problem of this game lies in the arc of the game:
- The first turn the choice is huge, because you have choice to put you houses anywhere.
- Then the turns after that feels limited because you have to put down houses next to where you have a house. That's why it's in the first turn important to put next to as little to different types of fields as possible. The thing is, that castles and towns, where you want to be first are adjacent to a lot of different types of fields (which does make it easier to connect to, but harder to connect from).
- The mid-game is more interesting as you have more choice, unless, you're like me and have drawn many of the same types of fields in the previous turns. In this stage you can finally work towards the goal.
- In the end game the board is getting cluttered up, so choices feel limited and it's more about searching where you can block somebody or try to get a point. But there are not a lot opportunities for this and there will be many turns that feel wasted because you don't score any points. So the excitement is gone and now you just feel obliged to finish the game.

All in all, fun in theory, but only really fun for a few turns in the (mid-)game.

RATING: 8
THIS GAME HAS NOTHING TO DO WITH DOMINION.
With that out of the way, this is a very nice logistic/placement game that reminds me of a cross between Carcassone and... Dominion. Not so much that you get to build up decks or whatnot during the game, but rather in the way the game is set up (random boards, which bring about random special buildings and randomly chosen ways of making points at the end of the game).
Very simple game which thwarts AP players by giving them only one card per turn (which is the terrain type your 3 buildings need to go on to) and plenty of time to think while the other players do their turn.
Very cool and easy to teach. Fast playing too with the right group and has that "one more game" feeling.
Recommended

RATING: 10
Own the Big Box edition with both expansions. 

I've played this game dozens of times with and without the expansion elements and I've concluded that I have very little negative to say about it. If you think the theme is weak, or the artwork simplistic, or the strategy shallow, well, I suppose I can do little to change your mind. 

I love the game for the variation on the map setup and the huge variety (even in just the base game) of the special abilities. Building for the randomized goals is always rewarding to me. I enjoy the nuanced set of choices afforded by the single card received each turn. 

For me, Kingdom Builder hits a sweet spot of strategy and simplicity. It's deep enough that I keep returning to the well, and simple enough that I can teach it to non-gamers. Its spot on my shelf is well-deserved.

RATING: 6
I have some problems with this game. 
Some are minor, like what was going through the minds of the designers when choosing the terrain types - canyons and ...flowers? Those are very strange to look at and most people will not identify them as such.
Some are bigger, like that you can get really screwed by bad luck of card draw and that you can get stuck in one corner, doing nothing.
But I still like the game for it's simplicity. You can play it with all kinds of gamers and non-gamers. The randomized set-up is cool, the special powers differ every game. Sure, after a few games you have seen them all, but the composition will most often be different. I will argue though, that not all special powers are equally strong.

RATING: 7
Interesting game.  Ignore the initial negative comments on this one, it's no where near as random or lacking in decisions as people are making out.  After your first game you can see far more easily the mistakes of placement that you made and how you could have played better once you understand the scoring.  I did MUCH better in the second game as a direct result of understanding how to manipulate my placements and the special powers that the tiles grant you to better effect.

This isn't the deepest game in the world but there's enough there to hold your interest over the short playing time.  I can see why people are lauding this as a great light game that could become another excellent entry into the gateway game stable.

Bear in mind here that I was ready to dislike this game based on the initial comments coming out of Essen and the designer - I think that Dominion is a very well designed game but I really cannot understand the love that it gets on this site, which has definitely given me a slight negative bias towards future games by Donald X. Vaccarino even if that isn't fair.  (I feel the same way about 7 Wonders.)

RATING: 6
Endgame tension isn't equally supported by all scoring cards. For example, Farmers (build settlements in all sectors and score the smallest) is Knizian, and worked well. Merchants (connect locations and castles) was tough to pull off, and didn't.

Sometimes the build-adjacent restriction really limits your creativity if none of the special tiles of that game allow moving settlements.

In short, a random combination of rules does not guarantee an interesting game. If I play again, I think choosing the special tiles and endgame cards deliberately would be advisable.

RATING: 10
After having enjoyed our game sessions with the excellent Dominion, Donald Vaccarino here adapts the strengh of the dominion license in a tactical placement game.

Kingdom Builder offers us to developp our kingdom by placing 3 of our little wooden houses each turn in a world of a patchwork of different territories.
There is only 2 very simple rules :
 - At the beginning of your turn, you draw one territory cards. You then place 3 houses on the territory type matching your territory card.
 - As far as possible, you must place your houses adjacents to any of your previously placed houses.

The world is made of 4 big tile randomly picked from the 12 of the core box. Every tiles presents a unique configuration of territories along with different abilities we could acquire to increase our choice of actions.
The goal ? Be the onr with the most victory point at the end.
Those points are computed according some 3 criteria cards  also randomly picked from 8 at the begginning of the play.

Thus, as in Dominion, configurations will always be different from a play to an another   but still respects simple rules.
Also as in Dominion, The game mainly remains on a important part of chance that has to be managed.

Indeed, it seems that you might only suffer the card you draw every turn. But all the sal of this game is in trying to place his house to optimize every possibilities for the next card so there will always be something interesting to do next. (and that's not so easy !)

In Dominion, the deck building does not mean to achieve a one time amazing combination but rather is to increase the probability to always get a good hand, or even to mininize the chance to have a bad one !
Likewise, Kingdom challenges us to be careful not to be stuck with bad cards because of bad placement choices.

As a conclusion, as a inveterate player of kingdom, I found in Kigdom the same feeling of optimization and management of chance than in Dominion as well as a effective yet simple way to renew every play.

A excellent game I recommend strongly !


In French :
Kingdom builder nous propose de développer notre royaume en déposant, à notre tour, 3 de nos petites maisons de bois dans un monde aux terrains bigarrés.
2 règles très simples :

à chaque tour, on pioche une carte nous indiquant sur quel terrain agrandir notre royaume.
dans la mesure du possible, les nouvelles maisons doivent être accolées à celles déjà posées.
Le monde dans lequel on évolue est constitué de 4 plateaux tirés parmi les 12 de la boite. Chaque plateau, propose une configuration unique de terrains ainsi qu'un pouvoir qui nous donne la possibilité de faire des actions différentes pendant notre tour.
L'objectif ? Accumuler le plus de points de victoires à l'issue de la partie.
Ces points de victoires sont attribués selon des critères tirés, eux aussi, au hasard.

Ainsi, comme son ainé dominion, ce jeu aux règles simples offre des configurations de parties toujours différentes.

De plus, de même , là aussi, que Dominion, Kingdom builder a une part importante de hasard que l'on doit tenter de maitriser.

En effet, à chaque tour, on parait subir la carte terrain que l'on a tiré. Or tout le jeu est justement de tenter de poser ses maisons en s'assurant de toujours avoir quelque chose d' intéressant à jouer quel que soit le prochain terrain pioché (et c'est loin d'être facile).

Ainsi que dans dominion on construisait son deck, non pas pour réussir une combo une fois, mais en essayant de toujours obtenir des mains intéressantes, Kingdom nous invite à faire attention où l'on pose les maisons pour ne pas nous retrouver coincer à jouer de mauvais coups.

Pour conclure, joueur invétéré de dominion, j'ai retrouvé dans Kingdom les mêmes sensations de calcul, de gestion du hasard et de tactique, ainsi qu'un renouvellement simple et efficace entre chaque partie.

Un excellent jeu que je recommande chaleureusement !

(Ps : un petit plus, le matériel est riche et joli ce qui incite encore un peu plus à sortir ce jeu souvent !)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The strategies that emerge from the interplay between location actions and goal cards is the true heart of the appeal of Kingdom Builder. With 8 different board sections (each contains only 1 type of location), 10 different goal cards, and endless board configurations, no two games of Kingdom Builder are the same. I played about 10 games over 3 days and every game felt fresh. We were continuously discovering clever interactions between the different location actions and goals. I played with both friends and family of wildly varying levels of experience with board games. Everybody picked up the game quickly and everybody loved it.

The focus on configurability, variety, and replayability will sound familiar to fans of Vaccarino’s groundbreaking deck-building card game Dominion. Despite the different formats, Kingdom Builder shares much of the same DNA with it’s smash hit sibling. In the same way that Dominion’s many cards allow endless combinations and reward on-the-fly strategizing, Kingdom Builder’s different boards and goal cards promise a unique experience every play. Both are also quick plays; Kingdom Builder takes about 30-45 minutes per game. Like Dominion, Kingdom Builder practically begs for “one more game” with different goals and locations, so you’ll typically play 2-3 games in a session. Both also hit a very sweet spot on the difficulty/deepness curve, meaning it’s great as a gateway game for the uninitiated, but will feel right at home on the table with your weekly board gaming group (even if you want to graduate to something a little more complex after a few rounds).

But, in many ways, Kingdom Builder surpasses Dominion by feeling even more elegant and timeless. There are less rules and exceptions (and text in general for that matter, the rulebook is a scant 6 large-type pages), but not at the expense of variety or strategic depth (well, maybe at the expense of some variety). I feel like Kingdom Builder will reach the table when I would normally pull out Carcassonne.


(full review: http://lookatthemoon.co/post/38834141417/kingdom-builder-review)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
(Rating including expansions: [thing=117793]Nomads[/thing], [thing=137396]Crossroads[/thing])

You never really feel like you're building a kingdom, but it's a fun abstract game in its own right. Or rather, a family of games rolled into one. The core gameplay of placing your settlements is always the same: Show your card and place three buildings on that terrain. But the sets of special tiles and the different sets of victory point conditions make every game different.

Having only one card also reminds me a lot of Carcassonne, which I really like. But, like [thing=822]Carcassonne[/thing], the base game can begin to feel a bit samey after a while, so expansions are definitely recommended.

On the other hand, having tons of different boards and corresponding tiles and tokens makes setting up and storing the game more laborous to a point where the lightness of the game almost doesn't qualify this extra effort. And this is where Kingdom Builder is inferior to [thing=822]Carcassonne[/thing]. Most of the expansions in [thing=822]Carcassonne[/thing] have little or no effect on the setup time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Kingdom Builder doesn't make a contribution to modern games in the same way as Vaccarino's previous effort Dominion did with its influential deckbuilding mechanic. Yet it is still a clever and excellent game that offers more that meets the eye.  Each game offers you a fresh challenge of goals, with a fresh combination of means to get there, and that will keep most players coming back for more every time again. While it won't satisfy gamers who are easily frustrated by some luck, there is more strategy than one might first think.  It's the game that works well for almost everyone, and deserves the generally positive reception it has received. A worthy Spiele des Jahres award winner!

See my comprehensive pictorial review: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/973335

RATING: 8
Only played a few times on cardboard, but hundreds of times electronically. I understand people's frustration with the occasional run of bad draws, but skill or in any case experience is definitely a factor, especially given the variation in points-scoring, special power tokens, and even the board. I love the quick, puzzle-y gameplay, optimizing the draw and effectively obtaining and using the special powers. It's a hugely-replayable light-to-midweight abstract, basically, that I find always ends just a touch too soon. 'Ah, if only I could have done XYZ... Alright, let's play another round!'

Update: Gaming crack. There's a fair bit of variety with which to become familiar, but the more I play, the less I lose. Relative to complaints of luck, with some setups certain powers become especially important, and it's true that if you get blocked out of them you're likely to lose. But so far that has rarely happened, or else you figure out a way to win despite it!

RATING: 10
[b]Light-medium game that is highly-variable (the board, the special abilities, the scoring systems all change from game to game). Highly-replayable light tactics game that is accessible for lots of skill-levels. Dry, but short and challenging.[/b]

Rating history: 10|8|6|6|8.....|6|4...|6|8......|10.....
Played 60+ times with 2, 3, 4 players.

APRIL 2016: After playing the app a ton in the past six months, and Dewald getting the KB Big Box, I decided to reacquire this as a highly variable weeknight tactics game. Very glad I did. The dryness is still there, but I'm enjoying it for what it is, and it is truly good for what it is. Also plays well across all skill-levels.

MARCH 2014: Nose dive. Just feels so dry once the novelty of new game wears off. Also, not appealing to any of the casual gamers I play with, so it's not getting played.

JULY 2013: I'm getting better at the game, but still won't rate a 10. It's like Cribbage and Dominion - doesn't wow me, but I'm always willing to play it for the challenge of playing well.

MAY 2013: I worried that this game would be too dry and abstract for my liking. Well, it *is* incredibly dry. It's also incredibly clever, and the multiple layers of modality (scoring, powers, terrain) make this one a home run.

My first impression was a 10, but the last couple games felt harsh with the large areas that are difficult to get out of. But it seems like clever play (i.e. multiple accesses) should mitigate that and maybe bring this back to a 10 for me. 

(Bought from Henry 40. Sold CL 35. Bought from BGG 35 (with Crossroads expansion 15.) Bought second copy from Dewald - Big Box 1 plus Marshlands.

RATING: 7.5
2012

This game had an uninspiring look to it.  The colors of the boards are atrocious.  Upon opening up, I was surprised by the beautiful art work on the cards.  I think the look between the cards & the board is incongruous.  But that is superficial.  

I again was surprised, because I really enjoyed the play which takes less than an hour.  Rules are few and simple. The board is easy to place pieces upon and score at game end.  The game changes depending upon the combination of various boards, and scoring conditions.  

I think and hope this game is going to get its share of play.

RATING: 5
The game is well produced, has a short play time (c.45mins) and simple to play.  Add to that the Spiele des Jahres award and on paper this is the perfect filler and/or gateway game.

However, I'm personally not sure how to rate this one.  I didn't dislike the game but i was left with a feeling that I wasn't playing the game - it was playing me.  It appears my first move wasn't ideal and subsequent repetition picking up the same 2 terrain cards repeatedly meant my moves were not my own choice.  There was no way to mitigate this at all (due to water and mountain spaces I was effectively landlocked) until I picked up a green card.  It took 6 rounds to enable a free choice action.
I know people will argue that the initial placement was poor and they may well be correct.  That said, how many other games are more forgiving of one single 'error' and will allow other methods of mitigation.

I'm not sure this is actually a family game.  It feels like a gamers game as I imagine it could be learnt like a formula and eventually mastered by some individuals.

I've decided on a 6 for now.  If I get to play again and learn the techniques a little better I may re-rate up.

RATING: 7
All too often games with only about half an hour of play time employ so many random and/or chaotic effects that there is barely a chance for good play to outsmart bad play. Kingdom Builder is different. Sure, the cards can still screw you at times, and it is in no way a given that the better player will triumph at the end but the odds aren't that bad.

The restriction of only one terrain card in hand is where it's at. Some complain that this means you have no choices and the game plays itself. I think they are missing the point. Learning to cope with that restriction and building your settlements accordingly so that you actually do have a choice where to go next is key.

The first Spiel des Jahres winner in a while that I'm eager to play more.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
"Kingdom Builder" est un jeu trompeur. D'abord par son format. Une grosse boîte à 40 euros, avec un chevalier et des châteaux dessus, ça fait plus "jeu pour joueur". En fait non, c'est un jeu très familial abordable dès 8 ans, et ce n'est pas mentir que de l'affirmer. Mais pour la personne qui ne s'est pas renseignée, qui en sait pas que le Spiel des Jahres est remis à des jeux de cette trempe, c'est trompeur et le regard se portera, je pense, sur autre chose. Autre blague, mais "Kingdom Builder" est loin d'être le seul jeu à la faire, c'est qu'on pourrait espérer se prendre pour des chevaliers bâtisseurs. Pas du tout, c'est un jeu de placement abstrait au thème complètement plaqué (sans thème d'ailleurs, plutôt), avec des cités sautant au-dessus de montagnes, des mignonnes petites fleurs...

Mais ce n'est pas bien grave, car c'est un bon jeu. Il se joue rapidement, les règles sont évidentes (d'où l'âge minimum pour jouer) : tu pioches une carte, tu poses tes maisons dessus. En plus, il a l'avantage d'être assez "tactile" et "visuel", on se représente bien l'évolution de la partie. Simple voire simpliste, donc abordable. Mais la subtilité et l'intérêt du jeu vient des règles de pose, qui apportent quelques contraintes. Et puis des objectifs, qui vont orienter le placement de chaque joueur. Et puis des actions spéciales, qui vont permettre d'accélérer la fin de partie, de s'étendre, de bloquer un peu les autres... Bref, plein de petites choses qui enrichissent le jeu de façon subtile mais efficace. Là, pour gagner, ça se complexifie un peu pour l'enfant de 8 ans. Mais oui, il peut y jouer.

La gros atout de "Kingdom Builder", comme l'annonce le titre si judicieusement choisi, c'est sa rejouabilité. Les bases vont toujours rester les mêmes, mais les parties vont à chaque fois être un peu différentes en fonction des plateaux qui vont donner des effets spéciaux différents et proposent une nouvelle organisation spatiale, ou des objectifs qui, par leur multiples combinaisons (au-delà du fait qu'ils soient différents), offrent une multitude de possibilités. Bref, ça sort souvent car c'est simple et accessible, et ça ne lasse pas pour autant, bien que le sentiment lors du jeu évolue lui assez peu. Les extensions apportent quelques petites choses intéressantes mais restent très superflues.

D'autant que, même si on a le choix de les acheter ou non (le consommateur ne l'est que parce qu'il le choisit), les petits ajouts donnent envie, mais en même temps ça prend de la place et on se dit que la plupart des nouveaux trucs auraient pu être dans la boîte de base (pourquoi ne pas mettre 5 joueurs directement ?). Et puis le prix, s'il n'atteint pas celui de la boîte de base, reste élevé. Mais bon, l'auteur devient coutumier du fait (on parle quand même de celui qui a fait "Dominion", jeu à extensions multiples qui ruine votre budget et font hurler votre compagne à cause de la place que prennent chacune de ces extensions...), donc c'est sans réelle surprise, d'autant qu'il n'est pas chez le même éditeur et qu'on peut supposer que cette politique vient de lui (mais ne nous avançons pas trop quand même).

Enfin, malgré ce phénomène qui a tendance à vite me rebuter d'un jeu, il faut avouer que "Kingdom Builder" est tout à fait calibré pour le Spiel des Jahres, a de nombreux avantages (accessibilité, rejouabilité, subtilités) et est presque idéal pour initier des non-joueurs. Un peu de frustration toutefois pour ceux qui n'aiment pas le hasard et qui sont obligés de poser leurs cités là où la carte le leur a dit, mais le fait de devoir s'adapter à cette contrainte n'est pas inintéressant. Un bon jeu donc, ni plus, ni moins, car malgré des qualités intrinsèques évidentes, le plaisir de jouer n'est pas aussi important quae dans d'autres créations ludiques.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A subtle tile placement game that scales well and affords excellent variety.
Decisions filtered through the single mandatory terrain type which must be built upon as randomly dictated by a deck are surprisingly robust. In the early game, players attempt to minimize the number of adjacent terrain-types so as not to be forced into building on one section of the board, while later players attempt to maximize terrain adjacency in order to expand as desired. This purportedly limiting one-card draw is the crux upon which this natural progression in placement goals is based, creating a great tension in taming its randomness.
The scoring conditions vary in each game, altering the value of the similarly myriad power tiles and even effectively adjusting each other's effective value as they can be pursued concurrently or exist in conflict.

(19 plays)
First Impressions: 7

RATING: 4.04
Okay, I agree the game has strategic choices, which may not be evident to first-time players (even second, third timers...).

I don't mind a bit of luck in games, unlike a lot of people on BGG usually do. However, IMHO Kingdom Builder feels like a game with very limited decisions, which are just not interesting enough (for me) to invest time on it.

Tried the (physical) game some times, the Android App many times, and uninstalled it.

The variable map setup remember Tobago a bit, which is a game I enjoy. The variable victory conditions are a clever idea. The "buy one card and play on neighbour terrain if possible" mechanism is very boring, and is the one thing you do most time. The game felt totally flat not just for me, but for the entire group as well.

Maybe we are "poor KB players" (surely are). Maybe we could insist some more, but there are just a bunch of games in the same style out there we would play instead.

RATING: 7
I'm convinced that this game gets an unfair rap. It's all about controlling your situation under extremely precise conditions. There's a big dollop of luck in it with which terrain card is pulled, but all can be mitigated through clever placement and judicious use of the special actions. It is more than a gateway game as I originally thought. It can get quite brain-burney at points! DXV manages to make a game that has an extremely simple turn structure with no fat whatsoever and yet still is a game with interesting decisions to make. Also, there really is no other game like it, making it a notable part of my game collection.

RATING: 7
(Very?) light boardgame which main pros are :
- short length (45 mns max.)
- super easy mechanics
- variable board settings + scoring conditions make every game a different challenge.

One (big) con : depending on the map/objectives mix, if your are "unlucky" to draw the wrong card twice or three times in a row, there will be nothing you can do but act like a robot, with no choice and no hope to compete for victory. Can be (very) frustrating at times.

Having said that, it should appeal to a large majority of gamers and non-gamers alike - more to the latter.
I can understand it was awarded the SdJ in 2012 in that respect.

RATING: 9

RATING: 7
Place tokens on a board to achieve a predetermined (but variable from game to game) set of VP conditions. An evolution of Through the Dessert with variable points scoring conditions plus a dash of randomness driven by the draw of terrain cards that dictate where you may place your house meeples - the game would probably rate even higher if they had used Knight meeples instead! Rules are easy to follow, the bits are good quality and the inclusion of 2x the number of boards plus 10 VP criteria (you will only use 3 in each game) gives a lot of variability to each play. Very good.

RATING: 8
Nice!  Super fast to learn, easy to play, fast.  Done in 20 minutes on first play.  Interesting and hungry for more.  Fits a nice sweet spot for parents with young kids trying to squeeze a game in during nap time.

UPDATE after 5 plays: *Audible Click*  We get this game now.  Lots more to it than the first impression reveals.  It's got depth for puzzle-solvers and point optimizers, and simplicity for beginners and kids.  Everyone can have fun, it's not so heavy that you can't carry a conversation while you play, and it's short enough that my wife asks to play two games back to back after the kids go to bed.  A winner!  We'll add Nomads and Crossroads to the mix soon.

UPDATE after 12 plays within a couple weeks! - Nomads adds the ability to gain some points during the game rather than only at the end, and increased mobility.  Crossroads adds more endgame scoring opportunities, and increased mobility.  And Marshlands adds an area control aspect, and increased mobility.  I appreciate that each add increased mobility, but of all of them, it seems like Marshlands adds the most to think about.  Now that we've played each expansion in isolation, we're going to go back to the base game and play the heck out of that.  Then gradually add the expansions back in.  Loving this game.

UPDATE after 24 plays: [Still just playing with the base game].  Introduced this game to first time gamers. Easy to explain.  Easy to teach while playing.  Great game for having a conversation, drinking some wine, and relaxing with friends.  I love how every game feels like a different puzzle to solve and optimize.  This is a real winner!  We haven't played a game so many times in rapid succession since we first found Carcassonne (our first board game).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Kingdom Builder has very few rules, but it's not as simple as it looks. It's a surprisingly nuanced, fast-playing, variable, addictive treat. This is a game of limited but subtle choices. You want to accomplish what you can this turn, while creating acceptable options for future turns. Like Dominion, it's a "lighter" game that's definitely aimed at gamers more than families. Like Dominion, luck of the draw plays a huge role, but good play usually wins out. Like Dominion, you need to assess the opening setup and get off to a wise start - otherwise you'll find yourself locked into a losing position.

RATING: 8
This game is the essence of the Goldilocks Zone for me.

It gives you options, but not enough to cause AP. It has plenty of strategy, but not enough to become convoluted. It varies from game to game, but not enough to be inconsistent. It has depth, but not enough to be hard to understand.

It's also one of the few games where my colorblindness gave me absolutely no problems thanks to *stellar* color selection on components.

An instant hit with the family.

RATING: 8
Not the best game in the world. A lot rides on which terrain card you draw. However, fixing this by holding an extra card in hand might significantly influence down time. There are also turns that you have to waste scoring few to no points because you don't have strong options available.

Don't really care, though. I simply love looking for the best way to use my 3 settlements each turn. I find the game a joy to play. The fact that it is easy to teach and very quick is simply icing on the cake. I see this getting lots of play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In your turn you place houses in order to get more points which are awarded at the end of the game. They are placed on one type of terrain based on drawn cards and you have to play it adjacent to one of your previously played houses if they can (if they can't you can play it on any place on the board with that terrain).The scoring is determined by three cards that are drawn at the start of the game and castles that are printed on the boards which you want to have houses adjacent to. There are also special abilities that can be used if you have a house placed adjacent to them, which really is what makes the game interesting.

If this game would be released a few years back it would be an instant success and probably a classic game as it is elegant and attractive. Now I think that people will find the game too abstract and the theme too bland. That said, I think that my experience with the game was good and would like to play it some more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[i]initial reaction[/i]
This is the latest game from Dominion designer Donald X. Vaccarino... and the first thing that's noticeable is that, like Dominion, there's a lot of variability in the setup... with a variety of boards and a myriad of different scoring cards, only 3 of which are used in any 1 game. There's also scope for plenty of expansions.. as proved by having one already listed on BGG (which adds another player, some new boards, more score cards and new bonus tokens).

So what is Kingdom Builder all about... it's primarily a sort of area control game in which players place their settlements on locations on the board according to the type of land card they have drawn...settlements must go next to existing ones if possible, but if you have no settlements on or bordering the land on the card you have, you may start a new settlement on that land type... this allows for clever positional play. It's also very important to pick up the bonus tiles, which allow you extra options for placement every turn. Scoring comes mostly from the 3 cards drawn at the start of the game, so tactics for any particular game will be dictated by which cards have been drawn... making for quite varied tactical gameplay.

Since you are stuck with whatever land card you draw 'at the start of your turn' there is a heavy luck element, and in the game I played my first 4 turns were all the same type of land.

RATING: 7.5

Some interesting combinations of scoring cards can cause more strategic planning than others. In first game we had Hermit and Citizen together, as well as Lords. That caused different players to choose divergent strategies and adjust upon seeing the others' choices.

Seems like it should have good replayability with the mix of scoring and boards / bonuses.

The high price tag for this certainly is another negative aspect. And, the usual expansion $ commitment to get more interesting options. I ended up buying the Big Box edition.
