RATING: 8
After 1 game I would have given this game a rating of at most 5 or 6.  But then I gave it a second, third and fourth try.  And from game to game, I realized more and more how deep this game is.  Many gamers seem to underestimate the number of tactical decisions this game offers.  But believe me: It's not just "draw a card, place meeples where card says".  A game of Kingdom Builder is won by the player who manages best to minimize constraints and who simultaneously opens up new placement opportunities.  The key to victory is to carefully examine the board before every move, especially when you can freely place a settlement.  A poor move on turn 1 can ruin the game for the next 3-4 turns.  Furthermore, the extra/bonus tiles are extraordinary important as they greatly increase your flexibility.  Bottom line: Great game which would be a worthy Spiel des Jahres 2012

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a strange beast: it is very simple and feels very light, but has some subtle strategies, and early mistakes may take players out of contention. There is a lot of variation with the special locations and the scoring cards, but not all of them or their combinations are equally enjoyable. It feels more like a puzzle and may lack tension, as there are few opportunities to interfere with other players' plans - not even a tenth of the interaction in "Through the Desert" or "Hacienda", which have slightly similar mechanics. But as the game progress and the players acquire location tokens giving them more options, more thought is required. It may be too long for a filler, but I have enjoyed it as a family game, even though the high level of luck of the draw keeps it from being more than that.

RATING: 8
Felfedezők és Birodalomalapítók
Előre le kell szögeznem, hogy első kipróbálásra nem tetszett a játék, de adtam neki még egy esélyt és ez jó döntésnek bizonyult, így az egyik kedvencemmé nőtte ki magát.
Maga az alapszabály egyszerű: minden körödben 3 házikóval bővítheted birodalmadat a húzott területkártya típusának megfelelően. Az építésnél egyetlen egy korlátozásod van: mindig kötelezően egy már letett településed mellé kell azt letenned vagy bárhova rakhatod a táblán. Miután elfogyott valakinek a 40 kis házikója, elindul az utolsó kör és utána a pontozás. Az alapfelállást kellően fűszerezi, hogy a táblán extra kis korongokra tudsz szert tenni, amelyeket felhasználva kombinálhatod a lehetőségeidet annak érdekében, hogy a végén a legtöbb pontot te szerezd meg. Az extra korongok segítségével egy körben 3-nál több házikót tehetsz le, más tereptípusra is építhetsz, mozoghatsz tengeren,… Ezek száma véges, így érdemes a taktikádnak megfelelően a számodra leghasznosabbakat már a játék elején beszerezni (amúgy is érdemes minél előbb szert tenni ezekre, mert az extra akcióiddal előnyre teszel szert). Fontos, hogy a játék elején húzunk 3 Kingdom Builder lapot, amely meghatározza, hogy a játék folyamán a táblán (amelyet a játék kezdetén a rendelkezésre álló 8 játéktábla részletből 4-et véletlenszerűen kiválasztva mi alakítunk ki) miért is kaphatunk pontot: tenger mellé építkezve, nagy településrendszert kialakítva, felfedezve az egész pályát, … A véletlenszerűen felhúzott lapok és kialakított játéktábla biztosítja a többszöri újrajátszhatóságot. Maga a játék is elég gyors, bár az analízis-paralízisben szenvedőkkel ez nehéz ügy, mert azért meg kell találni a legoptimálisabb pontszerzési lehetőségeket. A szerencse szerepe sem elhanyagolható, hiszen a körödben húzott területkártya határozza meg a lehetőségeidet és itt Fortuna kegyeiért sem árt imádkozni. Összességében remek játék!
A JEM magazinnak hála magyar lapokkal is játszható a játék és más nyelvtudás igénye már nincs is.

RATING: 10
Instant classic. This game is fairly unique for me in that every single move has an effect on the rest of your moves for the entire game. Made a few mistakes? Don't worry, it will be over soon. This is a common element in some very heavy strategy games but the difference is the game plays so fast you can just reset and play again. 

The first time I played with my buddy he was complaining about how he was getting screwed by the cards. I told him look, its not the cards its your strategy. Soon he started to see it. I explained that how on the iOS app I lost every game against the medium AI when first starting. Now I win 80% and the other 20 are usually within a few points. If the game were entirely dependent on luck of the draw it would have been 50/50 from the start and continued to be throughout.

As you progress into the game it takes a great deal of planning to beat an expert player. Every settlement you put down must be considered. Every additional action taken with care. There is no room for error once everyone gets to this level. I simply can't say enough about this game. I will note that I am a big fan of puzzles, and this definitely plays to that.

RATING: 5.5
A solid but uninspiring light train game.  The rules are simple, it is very fast, and the special powers and variable victory conditions are clever and provide a lot of replayability.  Frankly it feels like Through the Desert with a lot of the decision points removed, which could be a big plus or a big minus based on how much you like through the desert.  Kingdom Builder has two big flaws in common with dominion.  The title of the game doesn't match the game play, and after the first couple of turns the rest of the game plays itself.  I guess route builder didn't sound exciting enough but after hearing the title kingdom builder I thought this would be a light civ game of some kind.  Also after the first maybe three turns your board position is mostly locked in and each turn you are presented with an extremely limited number of choices, which are easy to optimize.  Dominion had similar problems.  

My biggest gripe about this game is the weight.  It is too light for serious gamers, with no hand management aspects and a very limited number of choices on your turn.  On the other hand I can't see busting this out with non-gamers since all the special actions, scoring conditions, and possibilities of card draw frustration won't sit well with them.  I think its in a bit of a no man's land and really only appeals to "optimization" gamers.  

The game design is solid as a train route game with variable victory conditions... it just doesn't overcome its own flaws.  Perhaps the expansions will add enough meat and potatoes to this meal to make it filling, but the base game just doesn't do much for me.

RATING: 9
Coming from the designer of Dominion, all the expected stuff is here. It's highly accessible, plays quickly, and features a setup that uses only a randomized portion of cards each time to ensure variability. However, there is nothing as groundbreaking as the deckbuilding mechanic that Dominion introduced. Yet it should not be unfairly overlooked.

Each turn, a player will place 3 of his houses onto the board, but must do so onto one specific type of terrain (randomized each turn). The arrangement of the houses will vary based on the randomized goal cards of the game. For example, you might get points for placing in a row, or placing on different sections of the board. Sometimes the goal cards will work together, and other times they will be in tension. It's a very simple rule structure, probably on the level of Ticket to Ride. And like Ticket to Ride, it gives players a decent amount of depth in terms of the choices they have to make. Players will need to watch what their opponents are doing and adjust accordingly, yet it is not a confrontational game; it is more about jockeying for position and winning various point categories.

Kingdom Builder has plenty to offer as a gateway game. It hits a sweet spot of simple rules with meaningful choices. In short, it's exactly what a Spiel des Jahres winner should be, and would be a welcome addition to the collection of any gamer who enjoys this weight of game.

RATING: 7.6
I think this is a good short game with great replayability.  With only 3 scoring goals (plus 3 points for building next to a castle), it's easy to teach and easy to focus on what's important.  I love that the scoring goals and maps are random each game.  Even this limited randomness (plus the randomness of the cards that the players draw) gives the game a good mix of coming up with a strategy at the beginning, and dynamically reacting to the tactics of the other players.

With only 40 houses, the game will end in at most 14 turns, and usually more like 10 turns, so it's about 30 minutes long with 4 players who know the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Connection games seem to be a right of passage for designers looking to make their mark on the gaming world. Not because "everyone is doing one" but rather because it's such a tried and true that has also become a trope that it becomes an interesting challenge to breath fresh air into this medium (especially after so many good games of this sort are already out there). Vaccarino achieves this in Kingdom Builder by proposing a short game which (importantly) inverts the game space: first, he provides only one type of terrain per move; and second, due to the way placements happen, you are invited not to expand your options too broadly (i.e., you are required to place further pieces adjacent *if you can* - key words). After that, the game feels Vaccarino-esque, there are variable abilities you can gain to build a bit of an engine, scoring opportunities against which you must balance 'engine building' with, and variable end-game scoring opportunities. This creates a short and sweet game which straddles between the unassuming simplicity of older spatial games and the contemporary bloat of SEPCAIL PWOERS! SECERT OBEJCTVIES! multiple special texts.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.66
There are a few games that are feel-good games for me. [thing=119432]Snowdonia[/thing] is one; [thing=40628]Finca[/thing] is another. [thing=122298]Morels[/thing] and [thing=123540]Tokaido[/thing] almost are but don’t quite make it.

Kingdom Builder and [thing=70919]Takenoko[/thing] hover right at the border of this boundary and I think the reason they don’t sit squarely within it like Finca and Snowdonia is the same reason Tokaido and Morels sit just outside it: depth of play. Snowdonia and Finca both incorporate their share of unknown factors, but they are decidedly susceptible to thinking ahead during play. Morels and Tokaido both allow degrees of thinking ahead, but their depth of play is foreshortened by their simplicity.

Kingdom Builder flirts with just this. It’s undeniably simple, and you certainly don’t play your cards: You play the board. The whole game is playing the board. Because you know that the cards will send you sandstorming across the landscape like bugs from an upright fan, sticking where you hit. So you want to aim where on the wall you hit. You want to make surgical strikes, in and out, darting in as close to a special bonus ability as you can and darting out again to another as soon as possible.

But everywhere you hit, you stick. And the more types of terrain you adjoin yourself to, the stickier you are. And where you stick, you ooze until you fill up the available terrain. Pray God you didn’t join yourself to another type of terrain, because there you are again, then. You want one way in, one way out, nobody sees, nobody knows. And since somebody always sees and somebody always knows, you’re stuck hopping into small ponds of terrain and out again, or toppling, Sovietlike, from terrain to adjoining terrain, sprawling across the board like a walking catfish drawn to nomad camps.

Scoring cards help with this. Once you consider that they (generally) simply identify quads or lats as additional locable goals on the board, you realize that they’re easier to hit than bonus tiles, and prioritize accordingly. But since bonus tiles let you rush the end of the game by placing additional settlements, it’s tempting to get caught up in the fun of placing a third of your remaining settlements in a turn. But if you aren’t placing them in scoring areas, you might as well have spared yourself the bonus tiles and concentrated on filling scoring conditions.

It’s a lot to think about for a game that consists in drawing a card and playing it. The game is what it is meant to be I think; it could bear a little more mental burden and move up into my group of choice feel-goods—exactly why [thing=188]Go[/thing] is at the top of that list—but I’m not dissatisfied with it as it is. As long as expansions keep on bringing more of the same, I’ll keep buying them.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]

RATING: 7
You must build 3 settlements on the land type you drew that turn. If any of your settlements are adjacent to that land type, you must build adjacent. As a result if you don't pay attention to where you build, you may find yourself shoe-horned into a particular area, building settlements that don't do anything useful.

It's so easy to get caught up in "I'm going to build here and use this token to move a house to there so I can get another special token" etc, but it's the goals that give you the points, not who collects the most special power tokens.

RATING: 7
A cute little game that gives me an idea of what a Donald X. game really is. I also enjoy the game's modularity, with the different cards and abilities. There are two drawbacks; I wish I could play the game with more than four, and I wish that it didn't have so much downtime--sometimes it does take a while to calculate out the possibilities. Yes, I realize these wishes are opposed to each other.

RATING: 6.95
Un juego abstracto razonablemente simple cuyas mecánicas principales (control de áreas, setup aleatorio y poderes variables) recuerda mucho a Hacienda o A través del desierto. Tienes que construir (build) asentamientos de tu reino (kingdom) para lograr PV's y "el toque mágico" está en las condiciones de victoria, que varían de partida a partida. No esperes nada complejo, rico o épico, porque es un Spiel des Jhares; más bien es rápido y fácil de enseñar y aprender. Me encanta el modo que combina 3/10 condiciones de victoria con 4/8 poderes especiales en 4/Muchos terrenos distintos y como la colocación inicial de los tres primeros asentamientos marcan tu ritmo de partida.  PROS: En función de las condiciones de victoria se juega como solitario multijugador o con mucha interacción. CONS: Sin tema. ¿Terreno "de flores"? Aleatorio y con mucho azar (tanto como Dominion, Backgammon o Yspahan). Al igual que Carcassonne o Alhambra, da la sensiación que es un juego bueno, pero que necesita de sus expansiones para ser interesante. Hacen falta 6 partidas para valorarlo bien y no todos están dispuestos a ello.

NOTA: es mejor barajar las losetas y escoger los tableros que no al revés. Da más sensación de aleatoriedad.

RATING: 6
The Dice Tower crew is wrong. Kingdom Builder is fun. I was very surprised, having avoiding this game for a long time after viewing unfavorable review YouTube videos. Kingdom builder is a gateway game level victory points optimization puzzle. A tasty points salad. Can I make better choices than my friends in the sequence with which I place my houses on the board? I enjoy this challenge. Kingdom Builder is now old enough to remove it from Dominion's shadow and evaluate Kingdom Builder on it's own merits. I enjoy how the board is setup differently every game with different bonus tiles and different victory points conditions--all of which provide tremendous replay value. Kingdom Builder has good production value with thick cardboard bits and attractive art. All of my favorite games are multiplayer victory points maximization puzzles. Kingdom Builder happens to be low on the game weight scale. It is a fun game. Lighter games have a place and play an important role; I don't want to play heavy brain burners exclusively.

RATING: 8.5
After a little initial skepticism, I've come to find Kingdom Builder a great abstract area control game (most closely related to Go in my mind). To make it enjoyable, it's critical to grasp the idea that you must keep your placement options open by restricting the terrain types adjacent to your settlements. Once you've mastered that, there's a world of strategies and combos to puzzle over. Well worth owning and playing regularly. Recently, I've been playing a lot on BSW.

RATING: 9.5
First – ALWAYS play with 2 terrain cards in hand instead of 1.  This makes a good game a GREAT one.
The original rule of playing with just 1 card in hand is absurd and the stubborness of Vaccarino sticking to it is even more so.

Back to the game: KB is an abstract, quick, simple (sometimes not) game with just right amount of decision-making compared to the playing time.  

I cannot emphasize enough how big replayability and variability is built-in.  Every game can be a TOTALLY different experience.
And I can imagine tons of future enhancements on top of that.  The game is a perfect base for them.

RATING: 5
2012-11-27

Initial Rating: 6.0 (November 2012)

This weekend I managed to play two new games from Vaccarino. Infiltration and this one. As I love Dominion, I was keen to play Kingdom Builder. I wasn’t expecting a heavy game as Kingdom Builder won the Spiel des Jahres this year (2012) and I was right, otherwise I would have been disappointed.

As a friend was explaining this game to me, I thought I would not like the game, however when we played it I found that I do like it. It is a very simple game to teach and learn, however it has more depth that I initially thought. It has a lot of luck of the draw, but I feel the player with the best strategy will usually win it more times than not. It still remains a light game, not one I want to play often but I would play it from time to time. The game plays fast enough.
 
Theme wise the game is very weak. As with Dominion and Infiltration, the game has a huge replayability factor, as you play with different goal cards and tiles each time, each game is different and that is a very good thing in my book.

For me, Kingdom Builder is reminiscent of Trough the Dessert, but with less tension as you have so many space to settle.

A game I will play again, but not one I feel any compulsion to own.
[hr]

I won’t pursue more games of KB, and I will offer some resistance to it. Most probably I will buy it for the iPad to play it quickly, but this is not a game I like.

RATING: 7
Kingdom Builder was a pleasant surprise of BGG.CON 2011 for me.

As anyone reading my other comments, or my profile, or my ratings, can tell, I'm not exactly the best test subject for 30-minute games.  In general I find the tradeoffs in depth of theme and gameplay necessary to get a game to that timeframe to be unacceptable to my tastes, and prefer 2+ hour games instead.

If any short game deserves a try it's one by Donald X. though.  And while I find the game play a bit too constrained by the terrain cards, the varying victory conditions ensure that Kingdom Builder will be highly replayable.  There are also interesting tactics around deliberately running yourself short on adjacencies to a particular terrain type to ensure access to new parts of the board.

A good game.

RATING: 8.8
Engaging fast-playing abstract with a nice touch of theme (think about the same level of theme integration as Carcassonne).  I like how Adam78 puts it: "This is an abstract game, with a beautiful atmospheric setting."

Huge game-to-game variability due do different boards, different powers and different scoring rules in every game.  I enjoy games that have "emergent properties" such as the overlapping trios in 7 Wonders and the organically created landscape in Carc.  This game has as nice an emergent landscape as any game I've seen since Carcassonne.  Beautiful, lush production by Queen Games, wonderful board and cards.  

Initially 8.2, upgraded to 8.5 after 10 plays, as it has really grown on me.

After 16 plays, upgraded to 8.8, liking this more and more.  I love old maps and historical settlement distributions, etc.  And this is a fun way to kind of recreate that, kind of like the "build a dungeon" game.  You can see how your peoples spread through the land if they were Miner Hermits, etc.  I like how lots of thought has been put into the terrain patterns on the boards so that they get put together in interesting ways; "valleys", chains of mountains, etc.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Kingdom Builder is a deceptively simple game, there is much more depth than may be apparent on a first glance.

The constraint of a one card hand seems needlessly punishing and limiting, but I would argue is the crux of the entire game. Setting yourself up to make use of any card is at the heart of good game play, and can be quite challenging. It also creates interesting design space for special abilities. By limiting player choice in which terrains they can access Donald is able to have a suite of subtle special abilities which would be useless if players could freely choose which terrain to place on.

Of course the system does allow for good and bad draws, and as such, players sometimes feel at the mercy of the draw. The more skilled you become at the game the less of influence this has.

There are two modular systems which create a vast array of variety between games. The first are the special powers in each game you use 4, and the second are the shared end game scoring conditions of which 3 are selected each game. Understanding the interaction between the powers and scoring cards is paramount to playing well. The value of a given power is affected by which other powers and which scoring card are in the game.

Each time I play the game I learn to appreciate it a bit more. I was fairly dismissive of the game after my first play. Now that I understand the hidden depths I am always willing to play. The game offers a lot of interesting decisions with a short play time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Upon reading the rules, I thought the game was too simple, too dry and a typical Euro. It kinda is, except that if you play multiple sessions at one go, you start to realise why its so good.

With constantly changing victory conditions, players have to evolve their gameplay style. If you play a single game, you won't be able to appreciate that the test of a players skill is in adapting to the changing maps and cards. You can never play the same way twice. I almost want to say "its like playing just 1 game of Dominion". The game shines when you play multiple times.

The biggest downside to this game is that you are quite often a victim of the terrain card draw. If there was a way to mitigate it, it would be a top ten game.

RATING: 5
I'm generally predisposed to liking games where you put tokens in hexagons, but I didn't really feel it in my first two games of Kingdom Builder. Obviously no one said we're talking about some sort of Chicago Express here, but it's not just the moments where it's thrust in your face that drawing one terrain type can mean all but a wasted turn whereas another would have showered you in riches, or how, when you've finally drawn exactly what you need, the wrong player turning up the wrong card can block you out of everything good, or even how, when these things have happened to you, they can [url=http://www.mspaintadventures.com/sweetbroandhellajeff/?cid=001.jpg]keep happening[/url]. You can learn to plan for all outcomes and secure flexibility, but then it seems like the crucial part of a game will be the initial race for whichever abilities match the scoring goals in force (try not to be seated last), followed by thirty minutes or more of sequencing the abilities you were able to grab... and in the end it still boils down to the cards.

There's an impressive array of modular elements, but while this is good for replayability it also seems almost like a cop-out: "Here, we couldn't decide which ruleset makes for the best game so cycle through all of these variants." Like an automated game construction kit that may or may not spit out a finely tuned scenario every time you press the button. Perhaps there should similarly have been a number of card drafting, hand management and turn order manipulation systems to add to the permutations.

Will play again to sample some more permutations.

RATING: 5

The special powers help a bit, and I admit that it was fun to try to figure out ways of circumventing the "must place adjacent to yourself" mechanic.  Still, though, I felt like there was very little I could do to change the course of the game for myself.

I thought I'd love it because of the comparisons to Settlers.  Alas, the only similarities were the presence of hexes and wooden houses, and the card style.  Kingdom Builder has none of the infinite variation and ever-changing strategy of Settlers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Kingdom Builder is a unique game, one that only gets better with more plays (dozens of real life plays and 600+ online plays so far for me).

With a first glance Kingdom Builder might seem a luck heavy game of placing pieces where the cards tell you to. This is far from the truth. In reality Kingdom Builder is a unique engine building game, where the goal is to build a situation (using clever piece placement and special tiles) where you can draw any card and still score big points. Once you get your head around this, Kingdom Builder offers both strategic and tactical challenges and plenty of tough decisions.

RATING: 5
I played my first game with the nomad expansion, but I am still going to make my review here.

It's a typical stuff placement/connection euro game similar to many other euro games. But the major feature of this game is the replability why I have to agree is interesting.

EAch game, you use 3 different cards that tells you how to score points. The configuation of cards you get will give you a COMPLETELY DIFFERENT game.

So kingdom builder is like a 20 game in 1 box. It's a simple non convoluted euro games that could probably replace a dozen of other games in your closet. So if it's you style of game, buy Kingdom builder and discard the rest.

Kingdom Builder is some sort of proof that most euro games simply glue mechanics together without any good reason. Kingdom builder does the same, but include all other games in 1 game. So it's like it said "I found your design trick and managed to include all your game into mine"

Finally expansions simply increase the possibilities this game has to offer by now making it a 50 game in 1.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow, I was surprised by how good this game is (two player at least, I have not tried it with more).   Addicting, different, lots of strategy, and lots of re-playability!  (although I can't wait for the expansion!).  It does NOT need any variants, the one card offers enough strategy and enough confinement to give a very interesting game with just the right amount of decisions, any more and it would be too easy to get perfect scores and your turns would be endless deciding where to go.  It has enough luck to make it fun, but enough strategy that the better player wins almost all the time, or all the time if they're a lot better :) .  I feel I need to play it more to learn all of the tactics and strategy it has to offer.  It reminds me of carcassonne in some ways, with less luck, but the same type of fun non stressful interesting play and the end scoring feels similar.  I think it might have about as much luck as Dominion. I love the artwork.  And I LOVE the fact that it's a simple game you can quickly learn the rules to, but has so much to offer in terms of strategy and fun, the perfect type of game! I am very impressed!!

RATING: 8

It's a wild-card that can be used for any normal terrain card, and can be used instead of the normal card in player's hand.

RATING: 6
You draw a card and attempt to put down three settlements on that terrain.  You are trying to satisfy various conditions in the game for scoring at the end.  There are other rules about gaining gold near castles and extra tiles for actions.  

The game is very abstract.  The name can mismanage expectations and you may think this is very different type of game.  However, it is light and family friendly.  It is a good game, but I tend like heavier games, and this is not one I would play repeatedly.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Rating based on one learning game in which I misunderstood an important rule regarding how to score victory points.  So, rating could change certainly upon further play.  But, that assumes there is further play.  Seems like Through the Desert with variable victory conditions and map layout.  Not terrible, but if I'm going to play a near abstract, I'd rather play Through the Desert.

Even from the one play, it seems clear that the decision-making is front loaded here.  After you study the randomly chosed victory conditions, you pretty much know where things are going for the rest of the game.  Dominion suffers from the same "puzzle more than game" issue.

Update:  Ok, now I've played it twice and both times did not understand the victory conditions correctly from the beginning.  This has not happened in any other game to my recollection.  I also feel like the game has the wrong amount of luck--determinative, but not enough iterations to even out within one playing.

Further Update:  Third time was the charm.  Finaly understood all the victory conditions from beginning to end of the game the same way.  Made for a much better experience.  Maybe still not a favorite, but a decent game I'll play with any group not subject to AP.

And yet another Update:  Well, actually, perhaps fifth time was the charm.  Had never played that you could take more than one of the same bonus action marker.  Apparently, still had never played by the rules.  This is the simplest game that I never play correctly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Simple and elegant game with endless replay value. The board is different each game but also the scoring mechanisms (3 out of 10 cards) vary from game to game. Each board have locations that give players abilities to bend the rules.

During your turn you have to play your only card and build 3 buildings on the terrain your card represents. You have to build adjacent to your previous buildings if possible and this is the key to victory: how you place your buildings opens and closes future paths on the game board.

There more I play this, the more I love it. Best with 4, but works well with 2 and 3 players (and 5 with the first expansion).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I was hoping for an occasion to give this a try without purchasing it. On the one hand, the rules intrigued me a lot, but on the other hand, there were quite a lot of disparaging remarks about the simplicity of the game due to the curtailed options during your turn.

After a couple of plays, I now know that I should have trusted my instincts and just purchased the bundle at Essen’12, because this one’s not only fast and addictive, it is also definitely a game of skill. Furthermore, the straightforward turns where you just play your one card occur once or maybe twice at the start of the game, and then the options due to special actions branch out immensely. And then, the game is over, and if you’re like me, you immediately want another go.

This will certainly be one of the very few games I’ll buy in 2013, the only open question is whether I can hold back on the expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
For something that's rated only a smidge over 2.0 in weight on BGG, it sure can burn my brain.  I've played 2 & 4 player games so far (35+ plays), but most games have just been my husband and I.  It's been interesting to see how we've changed our play and become much more thoughtful players as we've gained more experience in Kingdom Builder.  At first, I felt like 4 player was going to be best and 2 player felt like we were too isolated from each other.  Now, 4 player is definitely great, but we're much more in each other's face (when needed) in 2 player.  

At the start of the game, you have to take a second to consider how the locations, terrain, and point cards affect/play off each other - and how can you exploit all of that.  From that point forward, each turn will be a small puzzle - maximizing your placement for future turns while also balancing your chance to maximize your points.  

Definitely has a learning curve.  May come off a bit basic at first.  Most people aren't going to see the possibilities in their first few games and will likely be trounced by more experienced players.

Straightforward game play, plays quickly, and has quite a bit to consider each game.  I think I'm in love!

I will likely be getting the expansion - I'll just grumble a bit when I have to pay for it.

RATING: 10
4-26-14-Just a note to add that we play with all expansions in the mix from the time we received them.  There are a couple of things we leave out of Nomads; Miles does not like the tile that allows you to remove an opponent's settlement and also does not like the stones.  We love the way the expansions have kept the game fresh even after so many plays.  I would never choose to play without at least the Nomads exp.

7-29-12-To illustrate how much my husband (the non-gamer!) loves this game: he asked me today to order another copy because he's afraid we will wear out our current copy and then it will be out of print!  The only thing bad about this game is that I fear we will never get to play some of our other games that are still sitting in shrink!

3-8-12-We have played 41 games so far, in 3 weeks--we're obsessed!  Miles and I like to play 2p tournaments, best out of 3.  The more we play, the more we find there is to this one.  Sometimes you draw the objectives or the action tiles and you think, "we just had those in the last game!" but we've found that games are radically different from just one rotated player board or 1 card or tile.  We've also found that games are no longer 2p solitaire!  We are quite aggressive in our play and cut each other off when it suits us.  

2-23-12-We've found the games goes on too long (for us) if there are more than 2 special action tiles (of the 4 used for each game) which require players to move pieces from the board rather than from their supply. 2p games go about 15-20 min. and 3p not much more; maybe 5 min.   

I find it easier to get a truly random setup by shuffling the special action tiles face down on the table and picking 4, then finding the corresponding game board sections, shuffle them on the table and put them together randomly without rotating them again after the shuffle is done.

Sometimes, depending on the objective cards drawn, this game plays as multiplayer solitaire and other times we very much get in each other's way, cutting each other off from large areas of the board.  That even happens in 2p games!  I like both ways AND I find it lots of fun seeing what kind of game is going to result from each random setup.    

2-17-12-Right now I am obsessed with this game so I am making the guys play with it as often as possible.  So far we have 7 plays, either 2 or 3p.  I'm getting the hang of it a little better and am able to be more competitive.  It's great fun and the guys like it, too.  

2-12-12-Only played a couple of times but we are liking it.  It's not quite easy as some people are making it out to be.  Each decision is quite important even though the rules are simple.  I like the way that different objectives are used for each game and also boards.  All the variety should make this a highly replayable game.  It also plays fast which is always a good thing at our house.  I do see that this might be more fun w/4 players since then you would get in each other's way more, influencing your decisions more directly.

I like this game and have not yet played it enough to get any of the expansions, but Kingdom Builder: Nomads I am starting to make eyes at you across the room. 

A great family game that you can play with all ages. Experienced gamers need to get past the fact that you get told what terrain you have to place houses on by card draw. A good family game and definitely a keeper for now. I hope to get the first expansion soon and if I don't like that it may eventually at some point in the future drift into the trade pile, but that day is a long way off.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is an excellent game, cleverly tuned and nicely variable, with short turns that are enjoyable to plan without triggering AP. It's got a puzzle quality to it, in a good way, and it lasts exactly as long as it should. It feels very polished, and it's relatively easy to teach (even to non-gamers).

I love the way it combines 3/10 random victory conditions, 4/8 random special abilities, 4/LOTS of terrain placements, and random card draws into a whole that doesn't feel random, isn't purely tactical (you need to start with a strategy, but adapt it), and which feels satisfying without being too heavy. I've never played anything quite like it.

Although it's not a game where I feel connected to the theme, without the theme it wouldn't be nearly as fun -- despite being quite abstract. It plays differently with two (more wide-open, and you can often accomplish more of your original plan) than with 3+, but it's fun with all player numbers I've tried it with.

---

October 2014: Bumped up from a 9 to a 10. This is one of my all-time favorite games, delivering a consistently fun, engaging experience at every player count and with new as well as more experienced gamers.

It's also one of the only games I love to expand. I generally ignore expansions, but with KB they add options and increase replayability and variety without adding rules overhead. That's the sweet spot for me.

RATING: 6
Christmas Gift 2011

Plays quickly. I like it with three best. With four the downtime can be a problem as the game progresses while people try to maximize their "token" usage.

There are not a lot of informed decisions to make. Some of the angst you feel during the game is really a luck of the draw given what cards you'll draw on subsequent turns. With only one card in your hand, you can easily get cornered/forced out of contention on just a few turns if you're lucky enough.

My wife will usually play it with me which is a plus. Sometimes we'll play the same boards and end game point cards multiple times in a row (with a region card shuffle of course) just to see how the game turns out with different cards. In many cases there can be a drastic swing in points just based on the cards available.

RATING: 8
I was concerned that this would be too dry for us, and while it definitely is, we enjoyed it right from the start and more and more with each play. You can really screw yourself up big-time with your first one or two moves, but after some plays you have learned your lesson, and won't let it happen again. Those of you looking for theme should stay way clear, but if you enjoy the puzzle of squeezing out that one last, measly VP with a clever combo action, this game will deliver that in spades. The kingdom cards start to feel samey very quick and some of the cards / board combinations are not as interesting as others. I'm guessing that somewhere down the road I will have to throw in an expansion to keep it fresh, but not until I find one for a reasonable price (read: less than I have paid for the base game).

My first few plays left me lukewarm though, as the actual balance of the game is difficult to ascertain. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
UPDATE: Got in quite a few plays, good with 2, best with 3 I'd say (in respects to downtime in particular) - I downrated it a bit because it's getting a bit stale with the goal cards - expansion or replace I guess?

Mechanically simple, everything is held together by the one core rule - if possible, you must build adjacent to your existing buildings - and somewhat random (a card draw tells you where you can build). However, finding the best way to victory within these constraints is a very fun puzzle. I think I prefer a 3 or 4 player game, as these introduce some additional screwage you don't get with just 2, but either way it is a game that lets you feel clever.

I agree with another reviewer that the scoring cards are not made equal, some of them are "linear" in their scoring and these are to me a lot less interesting than the non-linear ones (Merchants, Farmers, Hermits etc etc). I typically find merchants, a linear card and one more non-linear score card to be the most interesting, and I'm very interested in getting the Crossroads expansion to have more score cards to explore.

I view this game as more a puzzle style game (less screwage) than say Carcassonne, but both of these share characteristics with the spatial element and the need to consider the probabilities - "What do I do if I need to build X now" and how to maximize the likelihood that your terrain (or tile in Carcassonne) is beneficial to you. However, the myriad of special powers you can gain during play means that you end up with a very wide range of potential "solutions".

While I prefer it more with 3 or 4 than 2 because of the screwage, I typically will only play it with 2, because with more players I will lean towards more interactive games which is not something this will ever be.

A great game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]01/24/2013[/u]:  I really like this game.  Unfortunately, as with many in my collection, I don't really get to play it with opponents, and that's why my rating doesn't quite make it to the next level.  I tried it out with my daughters a couple of times, and one time with all four of us.  I think it's a fantastic family game that has enough strategy to keep gamers interested.  However, despite the expansive sounding name, it's really an abstract at heart.  The theme is fairly non-existent, and, I think, that's where my family loses interest.

I enjoy the simple gameplay and the shear amount of variety involved.  You use four of eight available board tiles, each with their own special ability available to players, and  three of the available ten end-scoring conditions.  Some complain that your are too limited in your choices of play as you only draw one card per turn and you must place your settlements in that specified area.  But if you use the special abilities and do your best to place those early settlements advantageously, concerns with limitations should be rare.

I also love the aesthetics of this game.  Colors and colors everywhere.  The boards are full of seven different types of terrain (5 or 6 of which you can build upon).  The settlements are Catan style little wooden houses.  With the board full of settlement and landscape, I think it's really great to behold.  The learning curve is low for the rules, but the strategy is deeper than it appears at first.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I was prepared to hate Kingdom Builder. In fact, I had lost any interest in even trying it after hearing all of the negative reviews. I was talked into it at a con and I was very pleasantly surprised. It was fun! I like it best at two and three players, as the competition for location tiles gets too tight at four and five. If an opponent manages to get three or four location tiles to your one, you're at a severe disadvantage.

Some reviewers have called this an overly restrictive Through the Desert. I like it better than TtD for that very reason. Maybe I'm just not smart enough, but I find myself paralyzed in TtD, at a loss as to where I should play with the whole board open to me. Blocks are pointed and aggressive. I'm fighting a four-front war and losing. KB on the other hand hands you a direction, a restriction forcing you into tactical maximization. Longer term strategies *do* exist, both in the collection and execution of the location tiles and in more general terms, though you have to wait for the opportunity to jump on them. I love that the scoring conditions are random and change each game. In fact every turn feels like a delicious chaos: one of many terrain cards, some of many location tiles, some of many scoring conditions, now go win! It's not TtD or Go. It's light and chaotic and *fun*.

Future plays have reinforced to me the strategy in placement in planning for the future. Blocking yourself off, completing regions, avoiding touching other terrain, lots of ways to help yourself. Everyone I've introduced this game to has responded positively.

RATING: 8
This is very simple game. You take a card and place three houses on land hexes that correspond to the card. Some extra actions can be aquired as the game progresses. Last round is triggered when one player places last of his houses. There are ten goal cards from which three are used in each game. These cards determine how you should build your colonies to score victory points. 

Although this is very simple to learn it isn't boring or too easy. There is a lot of variation. Game board is not fixed but consists of four parts which are chosen from eight different board pieces available in base game box. 

I like to play Kingdom Builder when I want to have a quick fix to euro strategy fever and don't have time or energy to play anything heavier.

RATING: 5
Played this maybe 5 times now, mostly with expansion.  It's an average game in my opinion. Nothing that stands out that would make me want to own but then again nothing that would prevent me from playing if someone busted out at game night.  There are some simple assumptions built into game that result in a 'broken' experience if you don't consider them.  For example, if your first play connects with several terrains you could easily spend the remainder of the game in a feedback loop over which you have no control.  I have witnessed this occur and there is nothing that the player could have done to escape.  They easily could have skipped out on the next 2 hours without any impact to game.

That said, anybody that has played this game 3 or more times would likely avoid such a move.  My main reaction to the game really is just that I don't experience any emotions playing the game nor connections with other players.  There is a moderate amount of strategy to the game, but its questionable whether or not the reward is worth the effort.

RATING: 4.5
Kingdom Builder is probably not unfamiliar to any Cultist [of the New] on BGG. It's swept a lot of gamers away with the promise of similar replayability [or at least similar "interplay variability"] as the designer's smash-hit Dominion [which I find even duller than Kingdom Builder]. It's a relatively straightforward "tile placement" / connection game akin to Through the Desert, but using terrain cards to determine where players are able to place their pieces as in Hacienda or Attika. The gimmick of the game, and source of promised replay value, is that each game will have a randomly chosen set of 3 [out of 10 so far] cards that determine what will score points in the game at hand. Most of these are straightforward and uninteresting: "Score points for your largest group of connected tiles", "Score points for tiles placed next to the _______ terrain", "Score points for connecting certain locations", etc.

One of the scoring cards, however, is quite remarkable and is very interesting: the Hermits. The Hermits card gives you 1 point for every separate group of connected tiles you have on the board. "Big deal", you say. Here's the big deal: The rule for playing new tiles is that you get dealt a terrain card and have to put new tiles only on that terrain and, additionally, adjacent if possible to your previously placed tiles. If your previous plays are touching the terrain you drew anywhere on the board, you are required to play there, obviously extending a group rather than making a new one. This interaction between the placement rules and the scoring objective of the Hermits card is exactly the kind of thing that can make a game interesting; it's non-linear [i.e. you don't just get points for doing what the game causes you to do anyway] and it's truly difficult to capitalize on.

Unfortunately, the Hermits card is the most interesting of the set, and really the only one that has this kind of counterintuitive / counterproductive tension at its core. I can't see ever wanting to play the game without this particular scoring card, which makes me wonder why the designer didn't just make up a bigger preset scoring system that rewards this kind of interesting gameplay tension. Hacienda's market scoring, for instance, rewards similar diversity in position on the board, but the game makes it relatively easy to break up your groups of tiles so there isn't this tension [tension in Hacienda arises elsewhere]. Such a system could easily have been wrapped into a more consistently interesting game rather than left as a quirk that only shows up in about 30% of games. I'm told the expansion has other interesting scoring cards, but again, they turn up randomly.

This is the same kind of complaint I have against Dominion... it's just not consistently interesting, and the basic game structure itself simply isn't compelling enough to make up for a "bad" setup. In fact, this is also the chief complaint I have against another famously "infinitely replayable" game, Cosmic Encounter. Games need structural interest to work, and I don't see Kingdom Builder reliably providing that. I'd rather just pull something off the shelf that I know already has interesting tensions and difficulties built right in by the designer. I guess one could just always pre-select Hermits as one of the 3 scoring cards, but that seems contrary to the purpose of the game and doesn't make a lot of sense. The game still would need at least one other tension-producing objective that both opposed the normal, linear flow of gameplay and the preset "most separate groups" goal of Hermits. This is work that designers, not players, are supposed to do.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A scoring maximisation game that utilises some visual/spatial skills from the designer of Dominion.

The game generates high re-playability by using a combination of terrain boards and the selection of 3 Scoring cards that set the objectives for the players.

Each turn the players must place settlement (3) on a terrain determined by the drawing of card. What influences the players placement of settlements are the placement rules, which state that players must place settlements adjacent to other settlements already in play if they are in or adjacent to the terrain just drawn. What players are trying to do is keep their placement options as open as possible. 

It's a good game but does feel quite abstracted, which may appeal to some more than others. There is luck to in the drawing of terrain cards but the luck is lessened by a player's placements and their use of powers bestowed by key locations on the boards.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love this game. The main mechanism of placing three settlements every turn, adjacent when possible, is so simple, but of course, it leads to a lot of decisions that must be made. I like abstracts though, and I think the very abstract nature of this game, and the simplicity of its mechanisms, and its predictability is part of the appeal for me. I like the rhythm of it, especially. It's tense in its competitiveness but calm in its mechanics, and that's something that appeals heavily to me in a game. One other critical feature this game has is the randomized goals and player powers, so that despite the game's simplicity, it never plays exactly the same way twice.

I can see why some people do not like this game as much. The one drawback a lot of people mention is only being able to place on a single random terrain type every turn; I don't find that in general to be much of an issue, though. You can have some bad luck, of course, but limiting the terrain type does not limit your decisions that much since where your settlements go within a terrain type can still matter, and many decisions must be made about when and how to use the special abilities players can gain. The biggest problem this game has is that a bad starting location can utterly destroy a game. This is primarily a disadvantage the very first time one plays; after that, it doesn't usually come up, unless one chooses poorly. And, in fact, where to start one's settlements is part of the strategy.

For me the biggest drawback to this game (and is certainly not one that is unique to Kingdom Builder) is that you could in theory figure out everyone's score every turn and take that into account for planning - but that, of course, is time prohibitive. This, combined with some of the harder to predict or calculate scoring goals can lead to the final outcome of the game feeling too unpredictable. This is something that is mitigated to a degree by experience, but it is the biggest issue I have with the game.

RATING: 7
In this enjoyable filler, players first choose four boards out of eight to construct a map. Each board will contain different terrain, and extra action chits. By placing a settlement next to a castle, one can earn an extra action for the whole game. You also choose three victory card conditions from the ten available, so each game will be different. Your turn consists of playing a terrain card, putting settlements on that terrain, an dusing your extra actions.

It's very quick, and it does have a good dose of luck, since you only draw one terrain card. I like it, it has a "let's play another game" to it, though it may wear thin after repeated playings. But it's fun so far.

RATING: 3
[b]Artwork: 2/5[/b]: The review I had for this game last time stated that the board and cards look nice. I must've been drunk the last time I played this game because it looks like anus. The board is just an eye sore, though I will admit, one eye sore of a terrain looks very distinctive from the other eye sore terrains, so you'll be able to tell the difference between two different terrains easily. The only reason this isn't a 1/5 is because the card art is fine.

[b]Strategy: 2/5[/b]: I won't pretend this game is equivalent to Candy Land, but boy it isn't far. Only drawing one card heavily limits your strategy no matter what. I understand that there is some strategy, but that strategy really isn't that plentiful if the card you pull determines the action you can do, and most of the time those actions are pretty obvious. How... boring.

[b]Replay value 5/5[/b]: I'll admit, if you like the game, then the replay value is awesome. The modular board on top of the different objectives every time you play, very nice for people that enjoy the game. Not so nice for me, but I have to give credit where credit is due.

RATING: 4.5
My art partner asks why I haven't tried designing an abstract game.  I hold this game up as an example of an abstract gone wrong.  A modular board and modular win conditions?  Yes, it gives you replayability, but it reduces the exercise to a multi-player optimization puzzle... and frankly, if there's one thing the board game community DOESN'T NEED is yet another multi-player optimization puzzle.

I'll play it if it's your thing, but don't expect me to ask for it, which is deeply ironic given Don X.'s intention to create a game with infinite replayability.  You want an abstract with nearly infinite replayability?  Play Go.  A board, a lot of rocks, and about three rules.  And yet it stands the test of time.  I'm already bored with Kingdom Builder.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
After what I saw in some reviews, I was very afraid buying this game could turn to be a bad decision. They said the game was too simplistic, etc.

Definitely, it's a gateway game, but you have a nice deal of options/decisions/ways to play at your disposal. 

The game flows very fast, because you can think your turn in advance (the gameboard suffers few changes between your turns). We played our first 4 games (3 players) in less than 2 hours, that's < 30 minutes per game, 15 less than advertised. There are fillers that take more time! :D

I definitely liked what the game offers.

My biggest complaint is the lack of diversity with locations and characters (especially with characters, because it could have been easily solved). I guess it would be solved with expansions but so far, Nomads has not gone too far in this regard...

We played just 4 games, but we had many locations repeated and some characters too. There was enough diversity to make games different, but I see there is a limit there that will be reached when I play more games.

RATING: 6
I have come around to the (unintuitive and probably unpopular) opinion that the "replayability" in the game is largely illusory. The scoring cards dominate your objectives, but the single terrain card restricts your options so much that there is essentially no strategy, just tactical reaction. The 70 combinations of tokens devolve down to 2: ones that allow an extra placement, and ones that allow movement. yea, terrain will be random, and your cards will be random, but your algorithm for decision-making is pretty much only going to vary based on the scoring cards, and there is usually a dominant card that is going to be the most powerful (e.g., knights or merchant) that gains most of your attention.

I consider this almost more puzzle than game, and with 2 
players it has the advantage of being relatively quick.
-----
In Oregon at least - and I'm not really a huge fan of that game, either - you usually have multiple locations you can play to, often with ambiguous returns. You also have to choose whether to use your extra turn or joker, which can be a tough choice when the station and warehouse are not on the board. In KB, you're going to use your powers every turn, and there's usually an obviously best option.

I think there might be more 'strategy' in KB than Oregon, but like Dominion, it all pretty much occurs at the beginning of the game as you analyze the interaction of the scoring cards and board powers. The rest of the game is execution. Like Dominion, the in-game decisions are pretty trivial.

RATING: 9
Kingdom Builder is a reasonably simple Area Majority-style game, with similarities to Through the Desert or Hacienda. Unlike those games, it is perfectly playable for people who are colourblind. The board is modular, constructed of heavy stock and the rules are clear.

Kingdom Builder is light-medium complexity, and needs a little thought each turn. I found it easy to teach to complete newcomers: they enjoyed the game enough to play again, so I suppose it counts as a "Gateway" game too.

Play is pretty brisk. There are plenty of opportunities to block the other players, and enough variability in the boards and VP objectives to keep it fresh. [-]But it is not going to repay many repetitions in a short period of time; it is a light game and would begin to feel same-y fairly quickly. As a member of a regular rotation it is fine.[/-]

EDIT: That last bit was (a) wrong, (b) pretentious. KB is still getting played 2-3 times a month over a year after I first bought it. Top ten game with the expansion.

I really appreciate all three of these games for what they are and how they fit my game play moods. And all three are fun, unique and clever in their own right.

RATING: 7
心得:
不錯的遊戲
根據每回合開的目標牌來決定這場的得分點
然後地圖也會隨機設置可能到的特殊能力
主要問題可能會在每回合可以放建築的地點是用抽的
隨機性有時候會導致悲劇
策略性還是有的
規則:
1.每回合根據抽到的牌放三個房子在對應的地點
2.如果有能相鄰之前放的房子，一定要相鄰，沒能相鄰放才可以隨便放
3.特殊能力版塊拿到後，下回合可以用，如果之後沒連到特殊建築，特殊版塊就要丟棄，一個建築有兩個特殊版塊，拿過的除非丟棄不能重複拿
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent engine building game, similar to Through the Desert but where some of the locations give you special powers.

I quite like it, there's a nice mix of short term and long term tradeoffs. For example you could grab a power now, but that will put you next to a new terrain type or two, which could stop you being able to jump right across the board which might be important for certain scoring cards. But if you ignore a special power token, someone might cut you off.

Perhaps there is an optimal way to play each position, but the lookahead is so fuzzy due to cards and not knowing what your opponents will be able to do that it's hard to know what is really best.

The only major thing I dislike about the game is the card draw can screw you. You don't get many turns (40 houses ~ 10 turns?), so it's quite easy to get 3 or 4 copies of the same card and only 1 of another type. I'm not sure what I'd do about it - maybe a once per game wild card, or a Ticket to Ride style draught (hand size 1), or the option to dump your current card for a mystery card at some penalty (-VPs? fewer houses?) I think giving complete freedom is going too far, except [i]maybe [/i]on the first turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A positive vibe placement game. You're aiming to get your settlements out to score points, and the neat touch here is that the scoring rules are going to be different each game (3 of the 10 possible rules are randomly chosen pre-game, with no doubt more rules to come via expansions). Different games might want to build lots of small settlements, others big ones, connecting up locations, on different horizontal lines, etc, so there's a slightly different feel for each game which hopefully is enough to satisfy cravings for variety. The building restrictions (must always place adjacent to your existing settlements if you can) and the limit of 1 terrain card per turn (which may mean you can't place adjacent so you can go anywhere) both makes turns quick and also pushes you to place carefully and smartly. Getting connected to the extra action markers early allows you the opportunity to do clever moves, be it separating, moving or adding settlements before or after your auto 3 settlement placement on your terrain card to help you get where the scoring rules want you to get. It's easy to teach so the family can play, and there's enough there for gamers. Good light Euro fillery meat.

RATING: 7
Essentially a tile laying game with elements of route building and area control thrown in.  This is a game I liked at first, then grew quickly tired of due to the influx of new games.  When I returned to it more than a year later, I realized what a genius design it really is.  The mechanics are so simple and at first seem limiting (single card draw).  But this game, more than any other I have played, encourages and rewards players who are able to creatively shirk the restrictions and expand in an efficient way that best abuses the common goals that are available.  There is also a lot of decision making that goes into what abilities to collect and how best to use those abilities.

RATING: 4
Some combinations of board setup and ancillary powers work well. At its best, it clips along like a game of base-set Dominion. But some board setups and ancillary powers combinations lead to very odd results with slow, lumpy play and runaway leader syndrome.

Perhaps some play testing of scenarios and board setups would help? But just saying that the modular board and varied setups leads to "re-playability" is B.S. What varied setup really leads to is a mishmash of game experiences that range from good to bad.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
An easy-to-explain "area control" game of maximizing one's efficiency. Players place pieces to claim territory in ways that score the most points for that game, and try to claim "bonus action" chips that make adding those pieces more dynamic.

Certain strategies are dictated early (due in part to turn order), and sometimes, players repeatedly draw placement cards that they don't prefer (and, at times, can't use to advantage), so it's not chess or go (though it is, for all practical purposes, just as abstract as either).  There will be the occasional game where one player, due to a bad initial placement/tile disadvantage/scoring method combo, won't have much of a chance.

But it plays quickly, the leader is not 100% obvious (unless you repeatedly count up things before your turn), so the end-scoring usually contains a surprise or two, and it's tough to focus on all three variable scoring methods each turn, so most times you have a placement option even if it's not optimal for your primary strategy. For 45 minutes of game, it's pretty engaging, and with the bonus actions, there are some clever combos that can be put together.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A bit of a strange game, where placing is limited by which card you draw, but the more you limit yourself the more freedom you have. So strategically it's about finding a balance between maximizing your freedom and maximizing your score, while tactically you really don't have that many options. There's a lot of apparent player interaction, but usually there's not that much choice involved: you block others if you can and if it doesn't cost you too much, but it's not something you plan for. It lacks a strong core or some sort of 'story' that binds it all together, but that's because the focus is on variation from game to game, not variation within a game or robustness. Very game-y. Subtle deliberations rather than multiple-step plans.

Not enough control or narrative to play this once a year as the big game for the evening. But if you want some sort of 'bigger filler' that you can play often, that allows you to make a plan and then solve several smallish puzzles on how to best implement it, Kingdom Builder is very solid. 

It's pretty much perfect for what it is, which bumps my rating to an 8, but I do have to say that I'm happy owning big strategy games and small fillers, and I don't particularly need a game that's somewhere in between, not intuitive enough for an abstract and not narrative enough for a thematic boardgame. It's in a weird spot where I wouldn't mind playing this every week, but it also wouldn't bother me if I'd never play it again.

One more thing: I have a feeling it takes a couple of plays to get used to the fact that you only draw one card and you have to build on that terrain, and sometimes you will draw three deserts in a row or something, and perhaps you find out you had not considered that with your initial placement, and now you're frustrated. So introduce a houserule, like giving everyone an extra secret card that they can use once per game, and then drop that rule after everyone has one or two games under their belt. Normally you'd play a few games before you start thinking about houserules, but here the reverse might work well.

RATING: N/A
SdJ-W

+Variation: Modular board, Variable objectives

=Varying opinions on limited player choice; simplicity, less complexity but feels restrictive/fewer options (sometimes none) per turn =Expansion theme

Probably wouldn't win out over Carcassonne, Terra Mystica, etc

Own Android App

Post app purchase: I can see how the game isn't for everyone but I've definitely enjoyed playing it and think its a game everyone should experience. It's a puzzly area control game whose variability (boards, scoring cards, tile powers) keeps you reinventing your strategy and tactics every time you play. While it features a bit more luck/randomness than I like (occasionally you'll have a game where your every move is thwarted by the card draws), it's just about right to keep an experienced player on their toes and give a novice player a fighting chance. The blocking area control can get a little tense some games, but again it's just about the right mix that passive aggressive gamers thrive on. It does feel a little abstract and soulless (i.e Carcassonne seems more satisfying by completing features on the map, rather than making lines of identical buildings) but is great as a game that warms up the brain yet doesn't burn it out for the evening.

RATING: 10
Those who have been arguing that having only one card in hand makes it too luck-dependent are missing the point I think. With more than one card, there would be way too many options and you might as well just do away with the cards altogether. They're there as a constraint - if you play well, you should have sufficient options most of the time anyway. I'm really enjoying how the goal cards in play dramatically change the morphology of the board. It'd be kind of fun to photograph some endgame layouts and try to guess which goals were in play.

RATING: 7
You may not be building a kingdom, but you are certainly building routes. It is pretty abstract and very tactical. The card draw limits you to one terrain, which the placement rules forces you to fill up adjacent spaces. You are pretty much stuck in the part of the board where your houses exist. Unless you can touch the bonus locations to get some allowances to place additional houses or move houses already placed on the board. Having a few are very desirable. 

Change to play is provided by differing victory conditions and a gemorphic board. 

I can see why a lot of people don't like the game and think it is too random. The card draw is limiting, but the game is all about making the best of what you get and aim to provide as much flexibility as possible. While not a favorite of mine, I can see playing it occasionally.

RATING: N/A
I'm firmly in the camp who thinks there's way more game here than one might initially perceive (probably obvious since I've played it 48 times at the time of this writing), and I believe I've figured out what I like the most about it: it makes you earn your choices. 

There's a fairly standard expectation in gaming now where a game must shower you with choices to be good, or to stand out. The choices have to be difficult (or at least, [i]appear[/i] to be difficult) and a lot of enjoyment comes from valuing your options each turn, then choosing one of them. Kingdom Builder doesn't do this, and I find that refreshing. Instead, it guarantees you one choice. If you want more, you have to do something about it. The size of your decision tree is entirely up to you…

…well, almost. Yeah, you can be screwed by the card draw, but that's pretty rare (this is my most played game; there may have been two or three games in there where a player was totally hosed by the card draw). I think because we can set up, play, and tear down a game of this in 17 minutes (yes, we timed it) it doesn't bother me. I've reached a point where this is a filler for a few of my regular opponents, and that's a great place to be. It's been a good "let's play again" game for a while as well--with my father and/or brother, we generally play 3 times in a row.

RATING: 9
I am shocked and delighted at just how much fun my group and I have had with this. I've played with two, three and four players and love it with all numbers but it really shines with 3+

It's somewhat abstract and incredibly simple to teach and understand. Each play becomes deeper and more complex as the subtle nuances make themselves known like a fine chocolate melting on your tongue. The way different locations (powers) interact with various kingdom cards (objectives), the competition for prime locations and the luck factor that is just enough to add variety but not enough to be extremely frustrating. AP can occasionally be an issue, but not often and the speed at which this game can be played, reset and played again is a major plus for this highly addictive, beautifully designed game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A semi abstract, area control, area enclosure game with modular board, grid movement and route/network building.
Weight: Medium-Light.
Players: 2 to 5 players. Best with 3 or 4.

Impression: From the designer who brought us Dominion comes beautiful, light but thinky game where players are trying to spread their influence by building settlements (placing houses) throughout four corners of a kingdom, on six different terrain types and around mountains, lakes, capitols and structures that gives them various powerful, once per turn bonus actions. Settlements can be placed only on a terrain hex of a matching terrain card that is played AND only next to an existing settlement of the same color. This simple and brilliant design causes a lot of brain burning. For each game there are three different randomly selected scoring objectives. Also, in Big Box, which is huge, there is bunch of different terrain tiles, objective cards, bonus action locations and modules to keep game fresh for a very long time. And more expansions is on the way. Nice touch of the Big Box edition are the player pieces, which are houses of different color AND different shape. Components are generally pretty fine. So far the game was never not fun.

Likes: Setup variability: tons of different hexagonal grid terrains from which you chose four, different bonus action locations and different scoring objectives. Settlement placing mechanic. Wooden pieces differently shaped for each color.

Dislikes: Huge downtime with more players with nothing to do between turns, especially if the players are AP prone. Even planning is not viable because board state changes drastically during every player's turn. With more expansions setup and cleanup time is too long.

RATING: 5

In the end you also do not feel to have accomplished much, since you could build a certain number of houses each turn anyway. Additionally the art work is too colorful and the design in general not really appealling.

RATING: 9.55
Some people are a bit down on this game since it follows Dominion as the designer's second game and at first I get it why people are down on it.  This game takes a couple plays to understand that it is strategic.  At first it feels like it plays itself and you're just going through the motions but then after a awhile something clicks as you discover there is something very strategic and tactical to this game.  Yes it is random with one card draw but it's how you use this one card draw with the extra actions and what order they are done that really is the heart of this game which actually really for me has the same feel as Dominion  yet is a completely different style game. Great re-playability to this game with the different combos of boards and kingdom (objective scoring) cards.  You know a good game when you keeping talking about it long after the game has ended and this did that.  Just another great Queen Games game.  Another plus is that this games has simple enough directions that we'll be able to pick up and play it anytime.  

Update:  this game gets better and better the more you play.  We just keep discovering new strategies and the decisions just can be really difficult towards the end of the game however, the rules are just so simple and game play is still quick.  This game would be rated much higher if all the raters would play multiple times.  A game just blasting up my ratings and may not be done rising.

RATING: 4
Each turn players randomly draw a card representing a particular type of terrain. They may then play three settlements into terrain of that type anywhere on the board but adjacent to an already played piece if they can.
There will be three cards in play which have conditions under which settlements score points (for example: adjacent cities, adjacent to seas). 
At the end of the game the players add up their scores and the highest score wins. Its all very simple and very euro and I suspect the first player should win just about every game.

RATING: 9
Nice simple almost minimalist rules. Nifty varying powers and ways to earn points and map setup each game.

My only concern is whether the one element of randomness, namely the important terrain card draw each turn, is too much randomness. Possibly house rules can fix it if so. But so far we're enjoying it as is.

(UPDATE 2012-08-04: we sometimes play with my randomless variant: you simply choose your terrain type each turn. That works fine!)

I believe many negative comments are from people who only read the rules or only played once or twice and overstate the impact of randomness. The early turns are crucial and you can lessen the possible damage of the randomness, but that is not obvious the first time you play.

Played 4-er at Essen, bought it, playing 2-ers at home and continuing to enjoy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I heard that this was a disappointing game, and had trouble believing that the designer of Dominion could produce such a thing, though I suppose he had set the bar pretty high.  This has nothing at all in common with Dominion other than the designer, however, so it's probably not right to compare them... much.

This game felt simply wrong, and as we began I had trouble accepting that we were playing by the correct rules.  Draw a card, place a building on that terrain.  Try to fulfill the patterns which comprise the objectives for the match, which change each match.  It all felt contrived, and decisions seemed trivial to make.

I have read some praises, by those who enjoy and recommend the game, which seem to indicate that deep and nuanced play emerges with experience.  I stand by that the decisions, and completing the victory objectives, felt trivial to me, so I imagine that higher skill in this game must consist of evaluating all of the options - all of the different Forests you can play on, since you drew a Forest - and determining which one leaves the most favourable options on following turns, given what remains in the deck and what your opponents are doing.  That's legitimate, but if this is the attraction to the game, is this abstracted possibility-pondering more engaging than what you'd get from playing something like, say, Chess?

In any case, it doesn't matter to me, because what's lacking for me is any compelling reason at all to want to become experienced with this game.  It feels dry, there's no theme (just like Dominion), and the mechanics don't do anything to excite me (very unlike Dominion).  I'd be willing to play it again, but I'd rather play almost any other game that I've tried.
