=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Rating after 3 games. There is a lot I like about KB. I like the hunt for the optimal move, weighing the pros and cons of each placement. I like the emphasis on strategy, comparing the scoring cards with the randomized geography and planning all of your future placements accordingly. However, I have a single major concern. I'm concerned that when all players are at similar skill levels (usually the ideal situation for the best game), the end scores will be the direct result of who can acquire the *most* action tokens and who can acquire them the *earliest*. That's what the early game seems to be all about. And at even an average level of play, it becomes fairly straightforward to make a judgement about how to place your houses in order to accomplish the "most/earliest" goal given the terrain card you are dealt and any action tiles you may already have collected. In other words, I'm concerned that the outcome of the game may be too connected with early game luck. Certainly, you could say that all skill games have this quality (luck decides the winner with equally skilled players). But my preference would be for the design of the game to *obfuscate* this as much as possible. I fear that KB may not obfuscate it at all... making it very annoyingly obvious that a player won at the end of 60 minutes because luck allowed him to collect more action tiles and/or collect them earlier than other players within the first 15 minutes. For now, I will agree to play KB and my inevitable analysis of this issue will be the focal point of of my experience. I would say my rating is highly subject to change as I develop a more solid judgment on the issue.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Five different terrain types scattered throughout the land. Some cut off by water and impassable mountains. You build your settlement out three spaces at a time, trying to connect to cities and other powerful spaces that alter the basic placement rules. Three random victory point condition cards (drawn at the beginning of the game), give you varying goals to maximise your victory points from game to game.

The game is a little dull for my taste. The cards dictate too much of my placement decisions compared to more simplified games in the same genre (like Through the Desert), where my choices are more open and up to me. This would be a great gateway game for some, but it doesn't pique my interest. Although it's quick, I'd rather reserve precious gaming time for something else.

RATING: 5.5
Initial rating. 

I like it, I could get hooked on it, if I could ever get afford/borrow a copy. 

It's a spatial puzzle game of sorts where different players are trying to solve the same puzzle in a couple of different ways and blocking one another. A lot of game is about reading the scoring cards and adapting the strategy based on them, the special tiles and the spacial paths possible. Very simple to learn, but a grower - needs more plays to "get". As the game gives you one card you must use, the key is to position oneself so that any card could give you something of benefit (or just ignore this level and run with it).

My only problem is the visuals. There's no theme and that's okay, but the map is kinda ugly to me. The canyon, really? Not the best choice. Also the flower field looks kinda, ... wallpaperish. There must be a better visual way to represent this game.

RATING: 7
Ultra light tile laying game (reminds me of Through the Desert a little) where each turn, players play three meeples, I mean houses, to match their played terrain card. The game ends when a player runs out of houses. All other players play one more turn. 

Scores are tallied according to variable conditions determined by flipping open three cards (out of 10 total) before the game. VPs for building next to a mine hex etc. There are special location tiles where players can grab one of two tokens granting special actions such as the ability to move a tile two hexes, playing another house on a certain terrain etc. The hexes go fast and furious with three or four players and thankfully, analysis paralysis is reduced by limited actions and house placement each turn.

Not terribly exciting or nailbiting but clips along quickly enough that I wouldn't turn down a game. A minus for the HORRIBLE board design. Looks like a dog's breakfast.

SdJ 2012 winner? It doesn't quite sit up there in the pantheon of winners.

RATING: 7.2
Someone said that "from the designer of Dominion you wouldn't expect a total disaster as the next release". On the contrary, you *would* expect a total disaster, since they are famous now and nobody would bother to double-check the game for quality as long as they can put "from the designer of" onto the box. To be fair, it's not entirely horrible, and for, say, 10 EUR, I might even be willing to buy it as a filler game. I mean, you place houses according to some rules -- mostly "draw a random landscape and place three houses on that landscape, adjacent to your existing houses if possible" --, and try to fulfill some scoring rules -- in our case "place in as many different rows as possible", "have the biggest blob", and "place next to as much water as possible". It plays in 15 to 30 minutes, unless someone starts to micro-optimize. (You know who you are.)

RATING: 7
[2011-11-26]
Akkor kezdett érdekelni a játék, mikor egymás után több partit játszottak le egy mellettem levő asztalnál belőle és hangos dicséretek hangzottak közben. Tehát gyors és tetszik a társaságnak. A játék beváltotta az előzetes reményeket. Nagyon gyors is lehet, akár 20 perc alatt is lemehet egy játék és nagyon változatos. 8 térképrészletből sosem az összes játszik. Ezáltal a térképrészletekhez tartozó bónuszlapkák is mindig mások. A játékban elérhető pontok szintén sokféle kártyákon vannak, amiből 3 kerül minden játékban elő. Tehát ez is változatos, ami nem, az az egy darab akció, amit egy darab kártyán kapunk meg minden körben. Nagyon érdekes ötlet. A játék nagyon egyszerű. Különböző felszíntípusokra az adott egy kártya alapján rakhatjuk le három házunkat folytatólagosan. A három célkártya valamilyen módon díjazza a játékosok által lerakott házakat, amikor elfogy valakinek minden háza, a kör még lemegy és pontozás következik. Ennyi a játék. Akár gyerekekkel is játszható, nagyon egyszerű, nagyon változatos, nagyon jó taktikai játék. Egy 7-est adok rá elsőnek, de ez egy nagyon erős hetes, nagyon valószínű több játék után felfelé fog nőni a pontszám.

RATING: 7.3
I like Dominion but I am not a huge fan ... 

After only a single play of Kingdom Builder, I can state that i am much more impressed with this game than I thought I would be.  After reading about it and watching numerous videos I thought I would give it a try but fully expected it to be another game that sees limited plays.

My children and I all fully enjoyed it and everyone is looking forward to the next play, which is a rare thing as my children move into their teens.  Everyone is able to follow their own strategy and the "buildings" you acquire can tactically manipulate the board to improve your position and minimize your opponent's much more effectively than I imagined.  

The real bonus is the level of diversity through the variety of setups !

RATING: 7
Update April 2017
I respect this game, but ultimately decided to sell it. It is certainly deeper than meets the eye, but my tastes just kind of shifted after a while. I never really wanted to pull it out anymore. 
------------------------------------------------------

This is a light-ish game that is fast, fun, and easy to learn. My roommates and I have been playing it a lot since we've gotten it. We like to put the boards in odd positions which give a fun appearance. Sometimes we will use 2 boards to play a speed round. Variations are lots of fun. Not sure if it's worth the $60 price-tag.

It may have its flaws, but I still find it fun to play. The game does seem incomplete. I will be interested to see what the expansions will bring to the game.

As of now, it's an easy game to fit into my busy schedule.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I got this game for more than 50% off during a 30th anniversary celebration of a local gaming shop. I remember being interested in it but didn't know a lot about it other than the designer. When I first read the rules I was worried that it would be way too light and simplistic. This game has turned out to be a lot more interesting than it first appeared.

While your basic turn consists of laying 3 pieces onto the board in an attempt to score points, the combination of random goals and special powers and clever piece placement can create some really interesting strategies. Add to this some of the most impressive diversity between game sessions (due to random boards, random goals, and random powers available) and the short play length and I imagine I will be playing this game for a very long time to come. It's quick to setup and teach as well. Excellent game, especially for the (discounted) $20 I paid for it.

I guess my only complaints would be that you can really suffer from back luck if you get a few useless cards in a row and players who have played it more will probably beat new players very soundly, which may turn off new players...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The first time I'd played Kingdom Builder I wasn't sure how much I liked it. It was a quick play of a game that seemed to end shortly after it began. I knew I liked connection games, and I could see that Kingdom Builder had a nice mix of strategy and tactics, but I wasn't sure how deep it was. I wasn't sure whether the system was robust enough to stand up to many plays. But I wanted to play again to find out. It was different.

Fast forward two years. My tastes have changed. The board game industry has changed. One day I saw the base game and the Crossroads expansion on sale at clearance prices. I seized the opportunity. I'm glad I did. Whatever the reason, I like it more now than I did then. I get it now: when time flies by, as it did when I first played years ago, there's a reason.

RATING: 6
1 play, 4er

Oh, how annoying of a game is this?! The rules made it seem as if it would be fun -- a light, clever, quick little game with a lot of reply (randomly chosen boards/pieces/VP conditions every game, with a huge number of different combos possible). But then you play it and you find that you actually have almost no control at all ... the game almost plays itself! Yes, perhaps that a bit of an exaggeration, but with only one random card in hand, and the rule that you must play adjacent to previously placed houses, there's very little room for making actual decisions!

I doubt I'd play this with rules as written. But even something as simple as giving people a hand of 2-3 cards, or letting them draw 2 cards per turn from which to choose which to play (and discard the other?) would probably make this much more interesting.

--- Summary ---
Random board setup, consisting of 4 large boards covered with hex spaces of various terrain types, several city sites, and several action-tile sites. Randomly choose 4 special powers and place their tokens on the indicated spaces. Randomly choose some (3) end-of-game VP card. Deal each player a random terrain card (deck has every type of terrain except water).

On your turn, must take a mandatory action; may take optional action(s) if you've collected the tokens that allow you to do so. The mandatory action = one at at time, place 3 houses on the board:
- max 1 house/hex
- must match terrain of card being played (if possible)
- must be adjacent to any of your previously placed houses (if possible)
- if you place a house next to an action-tile space for the first time, take that action-tile (if any still present there)
The optional action(s) may be taken either before or after the mandatory one. These generally allow you to place an additional house:
- in a specific terrain type (determined by the tile) or in a specific location (e.g., edge of board, or in a line with 3 of your other houses)
- must be adjacent to any of your previously placed houses (if possible)

The game ends at the end of the round in which anyone placed their last (of 40) house. Score VP:
- 2 per city you are touching
- based on the previously revealed end-game VP cards (e.g., points for having houses in a row, or for connecting locations, etc.)
Most VP wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
A great puzzle that plays out quickly and differently each time, while having just enough theme to be the only abstract I have played that evokes a sense of accomplishment.  Luck factor is only an issue until you realize that luck is just another obstacle to overcome, and there are indeed ways to do so.  Collecting special powers is like a micro-engine building exercise, and the flexibility is using them before or after your mandatory action is what makes each play feel much more fluid and less rigid than the average abstract game.  It also doesn't hurt that, in my mind, this is as close as we are gonna get to Dominion: the boardgame.    

Very much undervalued by the BGG masses.  (And the masses in general.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an abstract game with a pretty coating.  The gameplay itself is super simple.  Each turn a player has a terrain type that he builds 3 buildings onto.  The buildings need to touch an already built building, unless they can't.  This makes for a decent amount of strategy behind such simple rules and gameplay.  The points all come from 3 goal cards that reward points based on different criteria and this will dictate where players are going to want to build their buildings.

At the end of the day this is just an abstract game and I'm not a big abstract game fan, they just don't excite me.  But there is still a lot of polish and a surprising amount of depth to this game.  The game still doesn't excite me but I wouldn't mind playing it if asked.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Very good family board game by Donald X., which may be a contender for the SdJ. Like with Dominion, you have to pick a strategy from the start and place your houses accordingly. If you play this smart from the start, there's almost no need to let your fate be determined by the cards you draw. The bonus tiles are very important for this and they need to be considered when picking a strategy. This gives the game enough to think about for hobby gamers like us, but it's still simple enough for families.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a quick playing, and ever-changing abstract game.  It's a super deal with all the expansions.  It's not a game that I find wishing I could be playing all the time, but I can't imagine turning a game down--the changing goals and set-up are just too interesting.

This is an 8 as a two player game, but the rating goes rapidly down as the player count increases.  At 5 I consider it completely broken...a 1, literally.  Unless player 1 and 2 and 3 draw absolutely the worst possible card, and player 5 draws the best, play 5 CANNOT win.  CANNOT.  Seriously, the 5th player cannot win with averaged luck.  That's broken.  In a 4 player game, player 4 CANNOT win unless the first two players have very bad luck and player 4 has good luck. CANNOT win.  Really.  In this respect, this is one of the worst Spiel des Jahres games we've played.

But it's good with two--excellent even.  Is solid with 3.  Broken with 4.  And don't even bother with 5. 

It doesn't play quick enough to encourage multiple plays to balance the turn order advantage--we'd rather play any single game for 2-3 hours, rather than multiple broken play-throughs for balance.  The game goes on WAAAAAY past it's interest-point.  You should probably cut the number of houses in half to 20 or 30 at most and just score it there.  The results, from the dozens of plays we've seen, would, quite literally, be identical to a full playthrough--player 1 or 2 would win, with players 3 and 4 competitive with each other, but strongly behind 1 and 2.

It may be fixable with a single-use, break-the-adjacency rule chit for player 3 and 4, maybe two for player 5.  I'll update my rating if that works, but so far...disappointing

Nope the problem is that you cannot "fix" the problem because sometimes there is no problem and then the "fix" breaks the game in the other direction, strongly favoring players 4 & 5.

I really want to like this game since the variable set-up and replayability are HUGE.  But the fact that a fair number of games are broken from the start is just BAD.

RATING: 5
Update 7/2012
Had to downgrade my rating slightly. Kingdom Builder is a fine game most of the time. However, I've run into a situation (multiple times) where I find myself in predicaments I can't escape. Meaning: I always draw the wrong cards and can't otherwise improve my score. Meanwhile, I watch my opponents run away with the game. Likewise, I've seen this happen to my opponents and *I* run away with the game. To be clear, there is room for tactical maneuvering -- some of the time. But when you get yourself "boxed in" it can be a drag.

One might say "Well, then don't let yourself get boxed in." This is partly fair. But it seems a 30-45 minutes game should have safeguards to prevent it being lost in the first five.

==

Doesn't feel like a gateway game to me. Maybe a few steps beyond the gate? I feel like there's more depth here than in Ticket to Ride or Carcassonne (both of which, I feel, aptly deserve the gateway label). I love the variable setup of the board and the unique scoring opportunities that will crop up each game. The game has a very pleasant air about it. Simple to play, getting a bit heavier as the game goes on, and ending just before it gets too heavy. Highly recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I played this at Essen 2011 and didnt like it at all. It seemed far too simple, with hardly any decisions to make.

With the iPad release I bought myself a copy and came to appreciate the game a lot more. There are important decisions to make, especially early on when you decide where to place your first settlements. Placing them semi-randomly will severely limit your options for the rest of the game. The extra actions you can acquire through settlement placement are also way more important than it first seems - it's not easy to see how powerful they are at first glance.

I still have 2 major problems with the game though:
1. Usually, if a game involves card drawing, you assume that the cards give you strategic or tactical options. In Kingdom Builder they are really only a randomizer which is completely counterintuitive. This misunderstanding frustrates a lot of people who find their expectations are not met at all.

2. The Dominion theme was obviously just tacked on for marketing reasons. Same theme, same author, good sales. It's a very abstract game really, the theme doesn't make too much sense. Only thing it does (besides generating sales) is, again, create expectations that it can't satisfy.

It's still a very good game, very suited for family play, but make sure to understand what it is and what it is not. Don't be fooled by the medieval card-drawing disguise.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Simple strategy game with variable boards and victory conditions. There are eight inter-changeable board sections and four of these are placed together at the start of the game. Three separate victory conditions are then revealed from a deck of 10 cards and the game proceeds. Players draw a single terrain card and then have to place their settlements on the matching terrain on the board whilst trying to meet the victory conditions, which range from receiving points from having settlements in a horizontal line, having settlements adjoining a mountain, having settlements separate from one another etc. The gameplay is extraordinarily simple but this game takes a fair bit of skill to play well, and not only that, it is fun to play. Factor in the ever-changing board and victory conditions and you have a game which does genuinely feel different enough each time you play it to merit several replays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Kingdom Builder reminds me a bit of Through the Desert, where players are building chains of pieces and connecting to points of interest. The main twist in Kingdom Builder is players build through various terrain that limits placement. This works well in Through the Desert, as players simply garner points, so a player with a slowly developing strategy can certainly catch up. In Kingdom Builder, however, players gain special powers at connection points that allow them to break placement rules and place more pieces. The problem with this, of course, is that players who are doing well gain the ability to do even better, leading to a runaway leader problem. The rich get richer in Kingdom Builder, and there's no way for a player who falls behind to crawl back into contention. In addition to balance issues, I simply didn't find the choices here all that interesting. An average game at its best, Kingdom Builder's rich-get-richer tendency thrusts the game squarely into the subpar strata for me.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/107529/kingdom-builder]Kingdom Builder[/url] has some much variables (in the objectives, in the draw of the cards, even in the composition of the board) and a game ends so fast (under 30 minutes), that is hard to make a proper evaluation of it. The game is basically a blur of fast putting of little settlements in the board then, caput, all is done. It took about eleven card draws for the match to finish and the end scoring issues.

In your turn what you do is:

- Draw a card (that shows in which type of area in the board you can put your 3 settlements);
- Put the settlements in the terrain showing in the card;
- Use the ability of one or more tokens you have (these you can take from the board after placing settlements adjacent to a port, to a city, and so forth);
- Pass the turn to the next player.

The game ends when a player is out of settlements (then play continues until all the players have the same number of turns). The scoring happens accordingly a set of objectives.

From what I read here in BGG, [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/107529/kingdom-builder]Kingdom Builder[/url] only start to truly shines after a plethora of plays, because then its details and tatics became more apparent. This might be true, and since a match is fast and the rules are so in the light side, I won't mind playing it more times to find out.

But, for now, my impression of the game is that it has no shine other than the beautiful components. I didn't find anything to make [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/107529/kingdom-builder]Kingdom Builder[/url] better than, say, [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/34219/biblios]Biblios[/url], [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/5782/coloretto]Coloretto[/url] or [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/56692/parade]Parade[/url], to name a few fillers that shares the same niche (fast playtime, easy to teach, good to great replayability) that I like better and are cheaper to buy.

RATING: 3
An underwhelming game. The artwork is boring, especially the map tiles, which are lackluster and look rather random. Speaking of the map tiles: when you use hexes in the first place, it doesn't make mich sense to use four big rectangular board tiles for any given game. This makes the setup much more repetitive than the hexes would have allowed it to be. 

Mechanically, you have very little influence on where to put your markers - the game makes most decisions for you. You can't really follow some sort of strategy; the game is much too random and leaves too little to the player. With relatively few turns in a game, the chances are high that you end up with an unbalanced terrain selection. The ways in which to interact with other players are minimal.

It's unclear what the little wooden houses are supposed to represent, or what any of the scoring conditions are meant to translate to in real world terms (why do knights want long lines of houses, along one specific angle of the board? Fishermen are happy to have a house on the shore, but a house on the water doesn't count for fishing?). The theme of the game could just as well be something else, or be totally abstract; the theme and the rules have nothing to do with each other.

If this was an iphone app for 0,79 EUR, it'd be okay. As a boardgame, it's boring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At the start of my first play I felt that my success would almost completely depend on the luck of the draw. It felt crazy that the game lets you draw, and then play only a single card - leaving you only the choice of the exact building spot. But then it became apparent that the meat of the game is exactly in that choice of your building site, because it determines your freedom of placement in future turns. In addition to that, the special action tiles combine with your mandatory actions and the scoring cards in an interesting way, making each game feel very different. Brilliant! Now I think that Kingdom Builder is an interesting family game that is quick to explain and play. There is still a chance that you will play a game in which the cards you get make it impossible to claim victory, but that does not happen often once you know how to make the best of your moves. I do feel that it plays best with the maximum number of players, but it is still great with just two.
UPDATE: The game's weakspot is definitely its replayability. After about ten games (in a short time frame), it was losing its appeal for me. This was fixed with the Nomad expansion and the two mini expansions, so for me these are pretty essential.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Base game rating 8, added xp rating 8.5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Not all that bad a game contrary to the naysayers out there. Heck, I like it more than Dominion. I agree that the game is simple and sometimes the strategy appears straight forward, but the choices are valid and can be excruciating if players are jostling for position and fighting to cover the same terrain given the scoring conditions. Some of the tile sets and scoring options appear to be more challenging and more "gamer-friend". For example, both the paddock and barn can alter movement of settlements to increase the options for expansion. Some of the scoring card such as horizontal and vertical scoring also adds a unique twist to conventional scoring. All said, while this game is not Le Havre or Agricola, it is a worthy SDJ winner given the audience. While the rules are simple, there are decisions to be made each move. Vaccarino is not my favorite designer by a long shot, but he is certainly the king of modular board game design. Kingdom Builder is a game designed for expansions, not unlike Dominion. By adding more boards with unique locations or just by adding scoring cards, you will have a completely new game. I generally reject expansions, in favor of a new game but I believe Kingdom Builder expansions will really enrich the gaming experience.

RATING: 7
Thoughts after one play: I like it OK. I don't mind the simplicity or the pick a card and play it -game play. Actually that part of the game is simple and elegant. Different scoring cards give nice variety in game play.

RATING: 9
I enjoy this game quite a bit. It's easy to learn, quick to play, and genuinely promotes experimentation. My group often tries to use Kingdom Builder as a 20 minute game to break up play sessions of much longer games, but we often forget our other game and play Kingdom Builder over and over. I have seen people criticize it for being luck based, which I think is a little ridiculous. Unless you are sitting at home playing 9 Men's Morris, Chess, or Risk every night, most games rely on some element of luck. Carcassonne, a much loved game, uses the same draw-and-play mechanic. The game is fun, and will continue to get consistent use from me and my friends.

RATING: 3
Too lazy to design a Euro for yourself?  Play Kingdom Builder!  You're guaranteed to get some sort of themeless mishmash of Euro mechanics.  It's like you're playing a real game, but without distractions like theme or mechanical cohesion.

At its heart, this is a tile-laying game, although the tiles are replaced by small houses in this iteration.  The core mechanics are rather staid, and the variation brought by the other mechanics is mostly in how the game is scored.  This is another entrant in the 30 minute throwaway game genre that is becoming increasingly crowded.  I don't doubt it will have it's place in certain households, but I don't care for it at all.

RATING: 6
Rated after 6 games. Feels like a good game, most likely has potential, but needs to be played more. The game itself is simple, but variety in the scoring might keep this one living for a long time. But this game needs players, who know the rules and the game and can play it fast. Otherwise this game is too thin, there is not enough content or satisfaction available, if the game lasts close to an hour.

I was not convinced after first four plays, but two two-player games made me feel better. Could be best with two players, when the downtime is minimal.

Played again after three years, this time with Nomads expansion and five players. It still feels a bit dry and downtime is quite big. Of course you can plan on others' turns, but others might ruin your plans. Might be "too easy" with two, but I don't know. I can play in the future, but not with newbies. Dropped the rating from 7 to 6 to better match my current rating scale.

Another five player play: Too much downtime, but I slowly start to understand why this game is so much liked. Also, I start to like this game as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
In Kingdom Builder, the players create their own kingdoms by skillfully building their settlements, aiming to earn the most gold at the end of the game.
\13\\13\
Nine different kinds of terrain are on the variable game board, including locations and castles. During his turn, a player plays his terrain card and builds three settlements on three hexes of this kind. If possible, a new settlement must be built next to one of that player’s existing settlements. When building next to a location, the player may seize an extra action tile that he may use from his next turn on. These extra actions allow extraordinary actions such as moving your settlements.
\13\\13\
By building next to a castle, the player will earn gold at the end of the game, but the most gold will be earned by meeting the conditions of the three Kingdom Builder cards; these three cards (from a total of ten in the game) specify the conditions that must be met in order to earn the much-desired gold, such as earning gold for your settlements built next to water hexes or having the majority of settlements in a sector of the board.
\13\\13\
Each game, players will use a random set of Kingdom Builder cards (3 of 10), special actions (4 of 8), and terrain sectors to build the map (4 of 8), ensuring you won't play the same game twice!

RATING: 8
This reminds me a lot of Through the Desert, with two key (positive) distinctions: the path to victory is explicitly different each game, and more importantly, even playing with 3 does not have the kingmaker problem TTD can have.

I suspect my rating will rise with more plays, since I also rated Dominion an 8 originally (now a high 9), and while this has very little in common with Dominion aside from the name on the box, it is similar in one key area: a random subset of the components are used for each game. I appreciated Dominion more once I saw how the different combinations of cards work, and I wouldn't be at all surprised if the same happens here.

One potential issue, however. During my first game, I misunderstood 3 key concepts about placement rules. As a result, I made 3 fairly sizable mistakes with my inital placements. I adapted my play style, and I did come in last, but only 4 points behind the first place player. This makes me wonder if the game is too balanced, as I certainly felt like I should've been much farther behind.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is not the spatial euro you're looking for....

Well, at least it isn't for me. Attika blows it away, for one. KB does do a few good things (it's short and punchy, and easy to set up and get going), but it's much less variable than it appears (the special actions aren't really all that distinct, and the variable maps are just there to prevent study, not to generate important distinctions in terrain). Which leaves the scoring cards, and they provided ten, which is too few by at least half.

As for the gameplay itself, it's just okay. The heuristics seem clear: leave yourself options, get 2 or 3 powers, work the scoring cards. I don't think luck is such a huge factor (and I certainly wouldn't want to play with a hand of cards or some no-luck variant), but it does seem like too many moves are obvious or forced. It's also very front-loaded, which is neither good nor bad in itself, but it sucks when you set yourself up for a 4/5 chance of a good draw (actually higher, usually, because of your t1 draw) and get screwed on t2.

I strongly suspect that, just as with Dominion, I'm going to like this game much more after a few expansions come out and give it legs. It's just an expensive proposition, and it's frustrating that it feels so truncated out of the gate.

RATING: 7
I backed this game on Kickstarter (the big box edition) and then asked someone in my game group to bring it along so I could decide whether or not I wanted to keep it. After playing it once, I realized that while I [b]do[/b] enjoy this game, I don't enjoy it enough to have it in my collection. 

I like the changing scoring conditions, the different maps, and, therefore, the changing abilities. I also really like the play time—45 minutes for a four player game is realistic. However, the game is essentially an abstract game and I'd much rather play a game that tells a story and/or has a narrative. 

I'll [i]mostly[/i] happily play this game when it gets brought out, but I don't need to own it.

RATING: 8
Lightweight, quick game.  First night we played this, we read the rules, did the setup, and were playing within 15 minutes.  Played another right after that.  Mostly an area control game, but with the added twist of different and random goals each game.  For example, there's a goal to achieve points by placing settlements in a horizontal line.  The rules are simple enough for casual gamers, so it makes a good gateway game, but the strategy is challenging enough for experienced gamers.  Even with 4 players it should play in less than an hour.  Downside is that it's an abstract game with little theme.  Upside is the replayability due to the random goals and modular board, and the interesting strategies that result.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The hype for this game got completely out of control (Donald X. OMG!) and there's been a lot of negativity and disappointment from people since then, but this game is good for what it truly is:   a fairly simple light filler game.

There aren't a ton of decision to make each turn but there are still enough to keep things interesting.   Drawing just a single card each turn can tend to make the game feel like it's playing itself, but once you get control of a few of the action tiles your options can open up considerably (especially the movement ones).


Gameplay aside, the components are very nice and I love the modular boards.  The way the special abilities are tied to specific boards helps with replayability because you can make a different combination each time, and putting the scoring track on the back side of all of the boards means it will always be available no matter which ones you're actually using.

While this game may not make it into the regular rotation, it's always still enjoyable to play.

RATING: 6.5
It's light and semi-random but quite enjoyable. The different scoring cards combined with locations really ups the replayability. I don't mind the card draw because you can position yourself to reduce the hurt from random pulls. People get in your way a bit more at 3+ players which makes things interesting.

Little too light, no desire to really play the game. I think the Nomads expansion would fix it up a little bit but I didn't really want to sink the money into it anyway.

RATING: 10
I prefer more complex, longer-lasting games, as they tend to provide more fulfillment than lighter ones, but I've found that Kingdom Builder offers a satisfying game experience.  It's simple and easy to teach, but it's still fun and will likely serve as a great gateway game for my non-gaming friends and family.

UPDATE: If you've only played the game once or twice and feel that it is too light, I encourage you to stick with it.  It's one of those games that improves with repeated plays.  Admittedly, though, it does play better with a lower player count.
